Preface
This Master Thesis was written in the Environmental Department of the Tech-
nical University of Denmark (DTU) and in collaboration with the Swiss Federal
Institute for Aquatic Science and Technology (EAWAG). The work has been car-
ried out from the 1st of March to the 31st of July 2015.
The supervisors of this project were Massimo Rolle (associate professor at
DTU Environment), Mario Schirmer (associate professor at the University of
Neuchâtel), Annette Johnson (Senior Scientist at the Water Resources and Drink-
ing Water Department of EAWAG) and Anja Bretzler (PhD student at EAWAG).

==PAGE:7==
Abstract
A sampling campaign carried out in 2014 revealed high-arsenic concentrations in
the groundwater of the gold-mineralized area of Poura. Because of the strong
dependence on groundwater for domestic needs in this arid region, it is crucial
to highlight the mechanism of the As geogenic contamination. Previous studies
focusing on local hydrogeochemistry determined that the contamination results
from the oxic dissolution of sulphide minerals present in quartz veins. In this
thesis, the groundwater system was assessed and combined with chemical mea-
surements in order to describe the sources and spatial variability of arsenic around
Poura.
To complement the chemical measurements from previous sampling campaigns
and measure the water levels and total depths of the wells required for hydro-
geological modeling, ﬁeld work was carried out in Spring 2015.
The water is
generally oxic and almost all As was present as As(V). The arsenic concentra-
tions in groundwater samples were relatively low, ranging from <0.5 µg/L to
317 µg/L. Five among 17 boreholes exceeded the WHO guideline for arsenic
(i.e. 10 µg/L). Two had concentrations higher than 50 µg/L. In comparison, dug
wells have As concentrations lower than 10 µg/L. Signiﬁcant diﬀerences were ob-
served between the groundwater compositions of deeper (boreholes screened in
ﬁssured/fractured bedrocks) and shallow parts of the aquifer (dug wells screened
in the weathered overburden). This likely results from the greater interactions of
deep groundwater with primary silicate minerals preserved from weathering with
depth. Similarly, the low As concentrations for shallowest depths are likely to be
the result of a depth increasing presence of sulphide minerals.
Data collected from the ﬁeld work and the recent conceptualization of hard
rock aquifers were used to produce a steady state model the groundwater ﬂow
around Poura on catchment scale. The model was developed using MODFLOW
and coupled with a non-reactive, conservative transport model using the parti-
cles tracking utility MODPATH. A digital elevation model was developed and
simpliﬁed vertical petrographical proﬁles were interpolated in order to deﬁne the
structural geology. The ﬁssured/fractured basement was considered as an equiv-
alent porous medium and the heterogeneities were then modeled through pilot
==PAGE:8==
vi
Abstract
points approach. Potential sources of arsenic were identiﬁed from the positions
of quartz veins reported in the literature and from ﬁeld observations.
Using the groundwater ﬂow model results, geological map and correlations
between arsenic and relevant physico-chemical parameters, the spatial variability
of arsenic is found to be primarily controlled by the lateral positions of the veins.
The exploration of the model uncertainty shows a satisfying potential to predict
contaminated zones, although mapping of the quartz veins representing sources
of arsenic was only partial due to lacking geological data. Moreover, the min-
eral assemblage is expected to change among quartz veins, making it diﬃcult to
identify precisely the arsenic sources. High-As concentrations are also expected
to be limited to the close vicinity (i.e. few hundreds of meters) of a vein source.
Indeed, considering the dominant oxic groundwater conditions, As is expected to
be strongly sorbed to the mineral assemblage while remobilization of As though
reductive dissolution of secondary oxides is restricted.
Current mining activities are limited to the smallscale of hand-dug tunnels
and pits on small scale. Although this activity puts workers at high risk and
requires more regulation, it was not expected to result in groundwater As con-
tamination. However, remnants of the past industrial mining activities exists
as ﬂooded abandoned quarries and tailings. These surface water entities were
found with high As concentrations ([18 ; 382] µg/L) which are likely to result
from the dissolution of As-bearing sulphides present in mine wastes exposed to
the atmosphere.
Considering that groundwater demand is expected to increase in West Africa
in the near future, eﬃcient mitigation strategies to insure safety of the ground-
water supply should be deﬁned. On local scale, drilling of As-safe wells could be
supported by hydrogeological modeling, although knowledge about the geology
should be gained and database accessibility for scientiﬁc research must ﬁrst be
facilitate.

==PAGE:11==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Arsenic (As) contamination of groundwater is a major public health threat en-
countered in many regions worldwide. A signiﬁcant increase in cases of cancers in
the skin, bladder and kidney (NRC, 1999; WHO, 1981) as well as dermatological,
cardiovascular, neurological and respiratory diseases (Smedley et al., 1996) has
long been noticed and reported where As contaminated groundwater is used as
drinking water. This pollution has been primarily attributed to a natural mobi-
lization occurring in diﬀerent areas and several geochemical mechanisms related
to the occurrence of As-rich groundwater concentrations have been identiﬁed. It
is now well understood that As release and transport are essentially controlled
by the oxidation-reduction and pH conditions (Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002).
For instance, the reductive dissolution of As-bearing minerals found on sediments
and occurring on large scale in Asia have been under focus of many recent studies
(Guo et al., 2013; Kocar et al., 2008; Rodriguez-Lado et al., 2013). In other areas
and more locally (e.g. Korea (Ahn and Cho, 2012), New England (Ayotte et al.,
2006), Alaska (Verplanck et al., 2008), Ghana (Smedley et al., 1996), Burkina
Faso (COWI, 2005; Smedley et al., 2007)), As-rich groundwater has been linked
to the oxidation and dissolution of As-bearing sulphide minerals.
The phenomenon of sulphide oxidation leading to As-rich groundwater is
frequently observed in West Africa where the basement is composed of crys-
talline rocks including the so-called hard-rock aquifers (Courtois et al., 2010).
Recent work has shown that the gold-mineralized zones in the Birimian volcano-
sedimentary sequence are particularly aﬀected by this pollution (COWI, 2005;
Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002; Smedley et al., 1996, 2007) as the ore mineral-
izations are usually correlated with the presence of sulphide minerals (Bourges
et al., 1998; Béziat et al., 2008). For instance, Smedley et al. (1996) reported this
pollution in a gold-mining area of Northern Ghana with As concentrations up to
64 µg/L. In a similar area of Northern Burkina Faso, COWI (2005) and Smedley
et al. (2007) have also measured As concentrations exceeding up to 160 times the
WHO provisional guideline value for arsenic (i.e. 10 µg/L) adopted in Burkina
Faso in 2006. The signiﬁcance and the density of the mining activities in West
Africa reveal the importance of ore mineralization and, consequently, shows that
As-rich groundwater is likely to be a widespread environmental problem in these
==PAGE:12==
2
1 Introduction
countries (Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002; Smedley et al., 1996).
While most of the studies concerning As-rich groundwater have focused on
young reducing sedimentary aquifers, research concerning the sources and mech-
anisms of transport of arsenic in West African mineralized basement is relatively
recent and the processes are not suﬃciently understood. However, in such arid
areas, groundwater from hard rocks aquifers represents the major resource for
drinking purposes although it is underexploited with a demand rapidly growing
(Barbier et al., 2006; Courtois et al., 2010). Recent drilling programs have led
in particular to massive construction of deep boreholes instead of the traditional
shallow dug wells. These changes in groundwater usage and demand reinforce the
need for a better assessment of this pollution by acquiring knowledge about the
sources and mechanisms of transport in order to develop an eﬃcient mitigation
strategy and to conduct better drilling programs in the future.
The district of Poura in the Mouhoun province of Burkina Faso is particularly
known for its important gold deposit and past industrial mining activities that
were carried out in the 80’s (Bourges et al., 1998; Béziat et al., 2008; Sanogo
and Prost, 1993). From sampling campaigns performed between May 2014 and
February 2015 by the Swiss Federal Institute for Aquatic Science and Technology
(EAWAG) in Burkina Faso, As concentrations exceeding the WHO provisional
guideline up to 30 times were found in groundwater in the vicinity of Poura mine.
In addition, Kagambega et al. (2014b) reported cases of soils and surface water
pollution in this area as well as the phenomenon of acid mine drainage due to
piles of mine tailings exposed to the atmosphere.
The present thesis aims to improve knowledge about the sources, transport
mechanisms and spatial variability of As in groundwater of West African crys-
talline basement through the case study of Poura. Based on the recent concep-
tualization of the hydrodynamic properties of hard rock aquifers, 3-D geological
and hydrogeological models were developed for a portion of the catchment in
the vicinity of Poura in order to study the likely As distribution together with
ﬁeld observations and the achievement of a geochemical analysis. Identiﬁcation
of factors that are expected to lead to high As concentrations were discussed in
order to formulate proposal for eﬃcient mitigation strategy.
==PAGE:13==
CHAPTER 2
Arsenic release and transport
in groundwater of West Africa:
a literature review
In the last decade, geochemical studies have brought knowledge about the pro-
cesses of oxidation and dissolution of altered As-bearing sulphide minerals as
arsenopyrite (FeAsS, the most abundant As ore mineral). This section oﬀers a
review of the geochemical processes that have been identiﬁed.
In West Africa, arsenic occurrence in groundwater has been primarily associ-
ated with Au-bearing quartz veins containing massive sulphide mineralization1
(COWI, 2005; Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002; Smedley et al., 1996, 2007). Nat-
ural dissolution of these minerals takes place when they are brought into contact
with oxic water (Smedley et al., 2007):
FeAsS + 3O2 + 4H2O →Fe(OH)3 + SO2−
4
+ H2AsO−
4 + 3H+
(2.1)
The speciation of As(V)/As(III) is primarily controlled by the redox condi-
tions, As(V) prevailing under oxic conditions as shown by Equation 2.1 and Figure
2.1 (COWI, 2005; Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002; Smedley et al., 2007). Note
that another possible oxidant for sulphide minerals under anaerobic conditions
would be nitrate (NO−
3 ) (Appelo and Postma, 2010) resulting from agricultural
activities and/or wastewater leaching.
The chemical Equation 2.2 shows the dissolution of pyrite (FeS2) and the
generation of hydrous ferric oxides (HFO) Fe(OH)3 occurring at pH>4 under
oxic conditions (Appelo and Postma, 2010).
FeS2 + 15/4O2 + 7/2H2O →Fe(OH)3 + 2H2SO4
(2.2)
1Arsenic is a major constituent in more than 200 minerals, mostly of ore type or their
alteration products. As concentrations in rocks, sediments and soils are generally low (Smedley
and Kinniburgh, 2002).
==PAGE:14==
4
2 Arsenic release and transport in groundwater of West Africa: a literature review
Ferric hydroxides, produced by the dissolution of pyrite and arsenopyrite, are
generally found on the surface of suspended sediments and of minerals in soils
and sediments (Wilkie and Hering, 1996). It can then tend to a more crystalline
form by undergoing slow transformation into minerals such as goethite, hematite
(i.e. ferric oxides). Both As(III) and As(V) are known to be strongly sorbed to
iron oxides/hydroxides over the pH range 4 - 9 (Dixit and Hering, 2003; Smedley
et al., 2007; Wilkie and Hering, 1996) and, to a lesser extent, to Al and Mn
precipitates, calcite and clays (Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002).
The common generalization stating that As (III) is more mobile than As(V)
is, however, too simplistic when such sorption sites exists. Indeed, as many trace
elements, arsenic transport is controlled by the pH, redox conditions, mineral sol-
ubility, kinetics and surface reactions (i.e. diﬀerence in sorption isotherms, com-
petition with other anions for sorption sites) (Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002;
Smedley et al., 2007). For instance, Dixit and Hering (2003) and Wilkie and
Hering (1996) observed that As(V) sorption into Fe oxides/hydroxides is more
favoured under acidic to slightly acidic pH (i.e. [4 ; 6.5]) than As(III) with the
greatest adsorption occurring at pH 4. Under acidic, oxic conditions, arsenic can
also coprecipitate to form secondary As-bearing minerals as scorodite which was
observed by Verplanck et al. (2008) in aquifer of an ore zone in Alaska.
Arsenic can then be remobilized as hydro-geochemical conditions change. For
instance, As can be released from the Fe oxides through reductive dissolution or
a change in pH. Therefore, Fe oxides are often considered as secondary sources
of As in groundwater or to retard the As transport (Appelo and Postma, 2010;
Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002; Smedley et al., 2007; Verplanck et al., 2008).
The presence of other anions (e.g. HCO−
3 , SO−4
2 , PO−
4 ) in groundwater would
also lead to higher As concentrations through the competition for sorption sites
(Dixit and Hering, 2003; Wilkie and Hering, 1996).
Although As occurrence has been primary attributed to natural processes,
anthropogenic sources have also been noticed through mining activities. The lat-
ter might enhance the oxidation of sulphide minerals when they are exposed to
the atmosphere through mine tailings or gold washing inducing soil, surface and
groundwater pollution. Moreover, the common technique of gold cyanidation to
facilitate gold extraction can also lead to As pollution and presents the risk of
releasing other contaminants (e.g. CN−, Hg) (Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002;
Smedley et al., 1996, 2007). In the presence of tailings exposed to the atmo-
sphere, the percolation of rainwater can lead to the formation of highly acidic
and polluted eﬄuent called acid mine drainage (AMD) (Coussy, 2012; Smedley
and Kinniburgh, 2002). Indeed, the dissolution of sulphide leads to production of
==PAGE:15==
2 Arsenic release and transport in groundwater of West Africa: a literature review
5
Figure 2.1: Eh-pH diagram for aqueous As species in the system As-O2-H2O at
25 C and 1 bar total pressure (Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002).
protons and for pH<4, ferric iron does not precipitate and can play the role of an
highly reactive oxidant (i.e. indirect reaction, Equation 2.3). Tailings and AMD
were already observed in the vicinity of Poura mine by Kagambega et al. (2014b).
FeS2 + 14Fe3+ + 8H2O →15Fe2+ + 2SO2−
4
+ 16H+
(2.3)
Strong seasonal climatic variations might also oﬀer a favourable dynamic pat-
tern for the release of As in groundwater. Indeed, the decrease of water level
during the dry season might lead to oxidation of sulphide minerals in the un-
saturated zone enabling the release of As directly to the aqueous phase or the
formation of intermediate As-bearing minerals such as scorodite under acidic pH.
During the rainy season, as water level increase, scorodite might dissolve under
neutral groundwater conditions, releasing the As (Verplanck et al., 2008). This
process is also reported by Smedley and Kinniburgh (2002) in tailings where
scorodite could dissolved during the rainy season forming eﬄuents.
==PAGE:16==
6
2 Arsenic release and transport in groundwater of West Africa: a literature review
In the ore zones of Ouahigouya in Northern Burkina Faso and of Obuasi in
Ghana, As concentrations tended to be higher for deeper boreholes drilled in the
fractured basement rock, the As concentrations in shallow dug wells being gener-
ally under the WHO guideline (Smedley et al., 1996, 2007). This was interpreted
as a result of a) a large amount of ferric oxides present in the weathered over-
burden, strongly binding As and b) of a lower residence time of the water in this
horizon than in the basement rocks. However, Verplanck et al. (2008) noted that
the groundwater residence time was not controlling the arsenic chemistry as no
correlation was found between groundwater conductivity and As concentration.
As the redox, climate and geochemical conditions as well as human activities
change among ore ﬁelds, it is not possible to formulate one common rule for the
release and transport of As. However, factors favouring the occurrence of high
As concentrations in groundwater can be identiﬁed in order to predict the spatial
variability of arsenic locally or regionally.
==PAGE:17==
CHAPTER 3
Site description
3.1
Location
The study area is located in the Balé Province of Burkina Faso, 160 km South-
West of the capital Ouagadougou. It lies between longitudes 2 ◦40’ W to 2◦51’ W
and latitudes 11◦30’ N to 11◦41’ N (coordinate system UTM zone 30 P North),
on the east bank of the Mouhoun river.
Figure 3.1: Location of the study area.
==PAGE:18==
8
3 Site description
3.2
Physiography, landuse and climate
The landscape of Poura region is mostly made of a large and regular planation
surface with the east side of the area boarded with some hills. The elevation
ranges between 230 and 330 m with a median of 266 m. The topography can be
divided into low, middle and high land surface units. The high unit is composed
of rare lateritic plateaus or scarps between 285 m and 330 m in height separated
from the middle unit by steep edges. The low and middle units form together
the planation surface gently sloping West to the Mouhoun river.
The lowest
elevations correspond to the two deep quarries remaining from the past mining
activities (Bamba et al., 2002).
Figure 3.2: Monthly averages of the evapotranspiration, rainfall, temperature
measured at Boromo (30 km N-W of Poura).
The climate is seasonal tropical and has a bimodal pattern shared between a
dry season (October-May) and a well-deﬁned wet season (June-September). From
measurements of the period 2003 - 2013 performed at Boromo, a locality close to
Poura, monthly precipitations are constant over the years. Evapotranspiration
greatly exceeds the precipitation over the year (i.e. annual precipitations and
evapotranspiration are 900 and 1800 mm respectively) except during the rainy
season (i.e. July-September) during which precipitation exceeds the evapotran-
spiration by a factor 2 (Figure 3.2). During the study period placed in the dry
season, rainfall is extremely rare although it can occur as short events from which
the water is lost to evapotranspiration without having time to percolate. Daily
temperatures range from 20 ◦C in the night to 45 ◦C during daylight (Direction
==PAGE:19==
3.3 Geology
9
Générale de la Météorologie du Burkina, 2014, personal communication).
The district of Poura counts about 10000 inhabitants shared among small
villages (Inforoute-communale, 2015) well distributed over the study area. The
main locality is Poura mine which is adjoined to Poura village. From 1961 to
1966 and from 1984 to 1996, important mining activities were run in the close
surroundings supported with the creation of the ﬁrst industrial mine of the coun-
try. The mining activity was stopped due to the decline of the gold exchange
rate although gold kept being informally mined. Indeed, industrial mining opera-
tions were progressively replaced by informal mining, the local inhabitants using
hand-dug pits and tunnels at depth ranging from 20 to 60 m (Bamba, 1996) and
processing the gold ore (i.e. gold washing) in various places spread over the study
area (Figure B.5).
The vegetation cover is an open wooded savannah (Bamba et al., 2002) which
is denser in the close vicinity of the Mouhoun river. There is also presence of
traditional agriculture that is usually performed at the end of the rainy season
(i.e. September-October) as well as traditional livestock farming although a large
part of the population has converted their work into mining.
3.3
Geology
3.3.1
Lithology
Poura is located on the Boromo-Goren belt (lower Proterozoic, 2300-2150 Ma)
(Bourges et al., 1998; Béziat et al., 2008).
The latter are zones of volcano-
sedimentary rocks variably metamorphosed and folded during the Eburnian pe-
riod (ca. 2150-2095 Ma) (Bamba et al., 2002; Kote et al., 2003; Ouédraogo et al.,
2003; Sanogo and Prost, 1993; Smedley et al., 2007). From a more detailed study
of the geological map of the site (Figure 3.4, the slightly metamorphosed base-
ment is mostly composed of andesite and andesitic tuﬀs with calc-alkano aﬃnity
(neutral volcanic rock) and of lower presence of rhyolite, rhyodacite (acid volcanic
rock). There is also minor presence of volcano-sedimentary schist and Tarkwaïan
rocks (i.e. conglomerate of coarse-clastic1 sedimentary rocks 150 m thick) and
minor intrusive tonalities of plutonic rocks that consist in gabbro-diorite as well
as ultrabasic rocks. The main structural features are the North - South strik-
ing verticalization of the stratiﬁcation together with a subparallel schistosity2
1Fragments
2Geological foliation of metamorphic rocks with alignment of the minerals.
==PAGE:20==
10
3 Site description
Figure 3.3: Simpliﬁed geological map of Burkina Faso showing the location of
Birimian volcano-sedimentary rocks and Eburnian granitoids as well as Poura
(Béziat et al., 2008).
(Bamba et al., 2002; Kote et al., 2003; Ouédraogo et al., 2003).
In the vicinity of the study area, a diﬀerent lithology is found. Eastern, it
is composed of later Eburnian plutonic granitoïds.
South of the Poura area,
metabasalt, a typical Upper Birimian unit, is found while the Western lithology
mainly consists in sedimentary rocks (sandstone and metaschist) of the Lower
Birimian.
==PAGE:21==
3.3 Geology
11

3.3.2
The fractured/ﬁssured basement
The crystalline paleoproterozoic basement of the study area as in the major part
of Africa is fractured/ﬁssured. Subhorizontal and subvertical fractures were origi-
nally explained by the structural stresses (lithostatic decompression and tectonic
forces) and rock cooling that took place during the Birimian-Eburnian period.
Recent researches have however invalidated this single hypothesis and showed
that the ﬁssures and fractures observed in the bedrocks primarily result from the
==PAGE:22==
12
3 Site description
long alteration and weathering process correlated to ancient climatic conditions.
In several studies, an higher density of ﬁssured was particularly observed at the
top of the basement rocks as a consequence of the weathering (Ahmed et al.,
2008; Carrier et al., 2008; Courtois et al., 2010; Dewandel et al., 2006; Koïta
et al., 2013a; Sharp, 2014; Wright and Burgess, 1992).
Table 3.1: Literature values of the ﬁssured layer thickness.

Table 3.1 exhibits some ﬁssured layer thickness found in the literature. It is
important to specify that the bottom of the so-called ﬁssured layer is more demar-
cated by a statistical limit representing the most productive part of the aquifer
(i.e. the useful thickness) than by a real geological limit (Courtois et al., 2010)
as the geology is closely related to the hydrogeological process. The thickness
of the ﬁssured zone highly varies between the ﬁeld locations as a result of the
diﬀerence in a) the succession of weathering and erosion cycles and the intensity
of these processes and b) the type of lithology (Koïta et al., 2013a). However, on
the relatively small scale of the study area, the thickness of this horizon is likely
to be regular as a result of the homogeneity of the weathering front (Dewandel
et al., 2006; Maréchal et al., 2004). Aperture and density of the ﬁssures also
generally increase upwards as a result of the higher intensity of the weathering
process, the few top meters of this weathered zone being generally characterized
by a dense horizontal ﬁssured sequence (Ahmed et al., 2008; Carrier et al., 2008;
Courtois et al., 2010; Dewandel et al., 2006, 2011; Maréchal et al., 2004; Sharp,
2014; Wright and Burgess, 1992). Figure 3.5 shows the recent conceptualization
of a geological weathering proﬁle. The bedrocks lying beneath this ﬁssured layer
are called the fresh basement and can be aﬀected by some rare deeper vertical
tectonic fractures (Carrier et al., 2008; Dewandel et al., 2006, 2011).
Important structural faults with a general North-South orientation are also
found in the study area and primarily aﬀect Birimian formations (Figure 3.4).
Heterogeneities as these faults or smaller lineaments (e.g. brittle fractures) could
1This value was reported in a ﬁeld 5 km away from the study site from the study of one
petrological proﬁle.
==PAGE:23==
3.3 Geology
13
Figure 3.5: Conceptual model of a partly eroded paleo-weathering proﬁle on hard
rocks (Wyns et al., 1999).
lead to a local enhancement and deepening of the weathering process at the periph-
ery of these entities (Dewandel et al., 2011; Sharp, 2014). Moreover, the appear-
ance of brittle and ductile shear zones in the various host sequences (metased-
imentary and metavolcanic) of the area has led to ﬂow of hydrothermal ﬂuids
resulting in the formation of mesothermal quartz veins and in the deposit of ore
and gangue minerals (Bamba et al., 2002). In fact, gold-free deposit associated
with the presence of most of the sulphide minerals such as arsenopyrite is pri-
marily hosted within many of these veins in the study area (Bourges et al., 1998;
Béziat et al., 2008; Kote et al., 2003; Sanogo and Prost, 1993; Smedley et al.,
2007).
3.3.3
The lateritic weathered overburden
Like elsewhere in Burkina Faso, the crystalline basement in the vicinity of Poura
is overlain by an altered lateritic cover (or overburden horizon) composed by
various stratiform horizons of weathered material although basement rocks can
rarely and locally show on the surface in the vicinity of Poura (Bamba et al.,
2002; Gueye, 1984; Kote et al., 2003). Petrological proﬁles of this weathered
overburden are shown in Figure 3.6. Note that lateritic soil is the term employed
to describe the deeply weathered reddish or yellowish soil containing clay minerals,
oxyhydroxyde iron and aluminium minerals and which is widespread within the
==PAGE:24==
14
3 Site description
tropics (Bonsor et al., 2013). It is the result of multiple changes in the tropical
seasonal climate occurring at large time scale. From bottom to top, the layers
found in the study area are the following:
• The saprolite (or alterite or regolith (Dewandel et al., 2006)) lies on the
ﬁssured crystalline basement. It is a clayey horizon, which results from a
prolonged in-situ decomposition of the bedrocks through weathering. This
process involves biogeochemical hydrolysis of the mineral constituents of the
rocks and their mineral transformations at new surface conditions. There-
fore, the formation of this material is closely related to the development
of the ﬁssured layer and its lower part retained the structure of the parent
rock (Bamba et al., 2002; Dewandel et al., 2006, 2011; Kote et al., 2003;
Wright and Burgess, 1992). The content of clay mineral (i.e. kaolinite)
generally decreases with depth while the size of the quartz grains increase
dividing the saprolite layer into an upper and a lower part, respectively the
alloterite and the isalterite (Bamba, 1996; Carrier et al., 2008; Dewandel
et al., 2006, 2011; Koïta et al., 2013a; Kote et al., 2003).
• The alloterite is also closely related to the thin mottled clay layer about 1 -
2 m thick observed in Figure 3.6 and which covers the saprolite. Its porosity
is partially ﬁlled with secondary clay ferruginous material composed mostly
of kaolinite making this horizon compact (Bamba, 1996; Kote et al., 2003;
Smedley et al., 2007).
• A ferricrete cover (or laterite or cuirasse or iron crust) with thickness usu-
ally ranging from 1 to 5 m forms a hardened horizon on the top of the
mottled clay (Kote et al., 2003).
It results from the ferruginization of
allochtonous material produced and distributed by the erosion of ancient
bauxitic plateaus that occurred prior to the kaolinic weathering process
(Bamba et al., 2002; Koïta et al., 2013a). Its thickness can in rare cases
reach 10 m in the area of higher elevations forming remaining ferricrete
scarps or hills. They are the residuals that resisted to the regressive ero-
sion and dismantling of ancient bauxitic plateaus occurring under arid and
semi-arid climatic conditions (Bamba et al., 2002; Kote et al., 2003). These
features form principally the hills located on the East side of Poura demar-
cating the Mouhoun valley. Consequently, the thickness of the ferricrete
layer is expected to decrease from the upper units to the drainage system
where it can be absent or covered by a thin layer of soil.
Close to the
mottled clay layer, the ferricrete consists in a thin layer of highly altered
assemblage of clay - ferruginous matrix called soft ferricrete which includes
Fe-Al minerals such as kaolinite, goethite and hematite as well as quartz
grains. From bottom to top, the ferricrete is more altered and, thus, more
==PAGE:25==
3.3 Geology
15
porous adopting, in the case of complete proﬁle, a nodular or alveolar struc-
ture.
Figure 3.6: Petrographic vertical sections of the weathered overburden found at
three locations of diﬀerent topographical units (So, soil; SFr, soft ferricrete; Mc,
mottled clay horizon; Sa, saprolite; nFr, nodular ferricrete; avFr, alveolarferri-
crete) (Bamba et al., 2002).
Bamba et al. (2002) reported discrete petrographic vertical sections of the la-
teritic proﬁle corresponding to locations in the vicinity of the study site. Figure
3.6 show signiﬁcant similarities between the proﬁles. This suggests a general ho-
mogeneity in the weathering proﬁle composition over the entire area. According
to Koïta et al. (2013a), the presence of Birimian formations (i.e. volcanic and
metasedimentary rocks) is linked with an important development of iron crusts
as well as a high content of kaolinite in the saprolite layer. This conﬁrms the
good agreement between the description of the lateritic layer made by Bamba
et al. (2002) and the geology of the basement. The presence of iron crust also
protects the weathered rocks from dismantling (Koïta et al., 2013a) reinforcing
the expectation of a preserved saprolite as well as a constant ﬁssured layer thick-
ness over the entire study area.
Alluvium deposits composed of gravels, sand and clay are also found over a
distance of 400 m on the banks of the Mouhoun river. It corresponds to the
==PAGE:26==
16
3 Site description
ﬂoodplain appearing during the rainy season and covers andesitic rocks (Aurouet
et al., 2005; Kote et al., 2003; Ouédraogo et al., 2003).
3.4
Hydrology and hydrogeology
3.4.1
Hydrology
The Mouhoun is one of the major rivers of the country and the only perennial
hydrological features found in the study area. It is ﬂowing North to South 4 to
5 km west of Poura and has a sinuous watercourse. During the study period (i.e.
dry season), its width is about 15 m within the considered area. As the Mouhoun
river is perennial while it takes source hundreds of kilometers upstream and ac-
cording to the seasonal arid climate, the scarcity of the contributing streams and
the topography, the groundwater is expected to feed the river (AEM, 2014). The
non-permanent Sambayourou river, tributary of the Mouhoun and ﬂowing East
to West is found about 8 km south of Poura mine and is therefore located outside
the area of interest.
Some relatively large open dug pits partially ﬁlled with water are observed
in the study area. They correspond to abandoned quarries of the past mining
activities and are about 40 to 50 m deep (Kote et al., 2003). The two major
quarries (i.e B1 and B2) are found a few hundreds meters far from Poura mine
and are respectively 5.6 and 2.2 hectares while a smaller quarry (i.e B3) is found
in the vicinity of Bologo (Figure 3.7). According to the depths of these quarries,
the water contained in it is likely to interact with the aquifer system.
3.4.2
Hydrogeology
In this area, as in large parts of Africa, the population is strongly dependent on
groundwater enclosed in so-called hard rock aquifers that are closely related to the
development of the weathering proﬁle (Sharp, 2014). During the time of the study
(i.e. dry season), the saturated zone typically lies within the saprolite and the
fractured/ﬁssured bedrock within the ﬁrst 100 m below ground surface forming a
composite aquifer. It is generally admitted that the groundwater ﬂow is enhanced
in the fractured/ﬁssured bedrocks (the bedrock matrix being inherently impervi-
ous) while the capacity of the aquifer is represented by the saturated saprolite
as it is clayey (Ahmed et al., 2008; Carrier et al., 2008; Dewandel et al., 2006;
Smedley et al., 2007; Wright and Burgess, 1992). Carrier et al. (2008) contrasts
this statement by underlining the fact that the saturated saprolite can present a
==PAGE:27==
3.4 Hydrology and hydrogeology
17
Figure 3.7: Hydrology and locations of the boreholes and wells in the study area
of Poura. B1, B2 and B3 are quarries partially ﬁlled with water.
higher transmissivity in the case of the aquifer having a greater thickness in this
entity than in the saturated fractured bedrock. Moreover, the transition between
the saprolite and the weathered overburden can be at varying locations sharp or
transitional regarding the density of the fractures as well as the clogging of frac-
tures by secondary clay minerals (Carrier et al., 2008; Wright and Burgess, 1992).
In order to collect this resource, two diﬀerent types of wells exist. First, tra-
ditional shallow dug wells with large diameter and having a depth ranging from
5 to 25 m receive water from the weathered overburden. Second, more recent
drilling programs have installed deep boreholes (Figure D.1) with diameter of a
few decimeters and depth ranging from 35 to 90 m receiving groundwater from
the deepest aquifer zone (i.e. the ﬁssured bedrocks). The locations of these wells
within the study area are indicated on Figure 3.7. Considering the predominance
of boreholes rather than shallow dug wells, this layer is expected to play a major
role in the hydrodynamics of the aquifer.
==PAGE:28==
18
3 Site description
The weathered bedrocks having a higher density of ﬁssures in its upper part,
conductivity is expected to be higher in this zone. However, as described above,
these ﬁssures can also be ﬁlled with secondary clay deposit which would limit the
groundwater ﬂow (Carrier et al., 2008; Wright and Burgess, 1992). It is also well
known that the heterogeneity in the fractures system increases the complexity
of the conceptualization of groundwater ﬂow in such aquifer as the aperture and
the connectivity among fractures spatially vary (Ahmed et al., 2008). Eventual
presence of dykes, quartz veins intrusion and tectonic faults (lineaments) in the
hard-rock aquifer also constitute impervious boundaries or lead to local enhance-
ments and deepening of the groundwater ﬂow. In fact, some of these features
might present a higher density of fractures in their vicinity due to intensiﬁcation
of the weathering process (Dewandel et al., 2011). In addition the fresh basement
underlying the basement rocks could be permeable locally where deep shear zones
are present although these features are of low occurrence and only present locally
(Courtois et al., 2010; Dewandel et al., 2006). However, regarding the relatively
low size of the study area, the aquifer can also be considered as unique with a
good connectivity between the ﬁssures. Indeed, the ﬁssured weathered bedrocks
are expected to be well preserved due to the good development of the weathered
overburden (Dewandel et al., 2006; Koïta et al., 2013a).
During the study period, groundwater recharge is expected to be non-existent
as the small rainfall events dispersed in time are quickly lost by direct or subse-
quent evapotranspiration occurring in the vadose zone. In fact, the percolation
of rain water is expected to be slow due to the clayey characteristics of the weath-
ered overburden. Recharge of the aquifer is more expected to primarily take place
during the rainy season. Note that during heavy rainfall small perched aquifer
can appear locally in the ferricrete layer (Carrier et al., 2008; Sharp, 2014).
==PAGE:29==
CHAPTER 4
Methods
4.1
Digital elevation model
An accurate description of the terrain surface is obtained by generating a digital
elevation model (DEM) of the area of interest. In fact, a DEM gives a quanti-
tative representation of the earth’s surface by providing the elevation value for
a particular location. Therefore, it allows an assessment of the groundwater sys-
tem by the study of the topography as well as the conversion of the measured
groundwater levels into hydraulic heads.
4.1.1
Choice of the DEM data
The ﬁrst near-gobal high-resolution DEM produced by synthetic aperture radar
during the NASA’s Shuttle Radar Topography Mission (SRTM) is used to gener-
ate the DEM of the area. The SRTM data consist in a recent open source DEM
of Earth oﬀering wide range of possibilities for modeling. It is obtained in raster
format which consists of a set of square cells, each containing a speciﬁc elevation
value resulting in a continuous spatial coverage of the area. SRTM data is ref-
erenced horizontally to the WGS84 datum and vertically to the EGM96 geoid
reference surface (Mukherjee et al., 2013; Slater et al., 2006).
The 1 Arc-Second SRTM Global elevation data have an horizontal resolution
of 30 m and a vertical resolution of 1 m. The speciﬁed accuracy requirements
from the NASA were less than 16 m absolute vertical error (90 percent linear er-
ror), 20 m absolute horizontal error (90 percent circular error) and 10 m relative
vertical error. Elevation errors in the SRTM product is mostly due to speckling
or system noise in the form of spikes and wells although large voids and anoma-
lous points are automatically corrected during the SRTM data editing process
(Falorni et al., 2005; Slater et al., 2006). Falorni et al. (2005), Mukherjee et al.
(2013), and Slater et al. (2006) veriﬁed the vertical accuracy of the 1-arc SRTM
data at regional scale and found that it signiﬁcantly exceeds the requirements for
area of low slopes and low altitudes. For instance, Slater et al. (2006) reported
an absolute vertical accuracy of 7 m or less (91.7 percent linear error) and of 10
m or less (99.4 percent linear error) with all cells having vertical accuracy values
==PAGE:30==
20
4 Methods
falling between 3 and 15 meters. In addition, limited amount of data voids and
limited speckling are expected in the study area as steep slopes and water bodies
are not signiﬁcantly present (Falorni et al., 2005).
Vertical errors due to the
presence of vegetation and buildings are considered insigniﬁcant as these features
are rare over the study area.
Considering the general ﬂat topography of the terrain, presence of noise could
signiﬁcantly impact the modeled groundwater direction ﬂow by negatively aﬀect-
ing a) the piezometric levels converted from the measured water levels (Section
4.5) and b) the conceptual geological model derived from the terrain elevation
(Section 4.7).
Modeled hydrogeology is particularly known to be sensitive to
the modeled land surface morphology as the water table is in general closely re-
lated to it (Kuniansky et al., 2009). Thus, noise should be removed in order
to allow the DEM to provide an accurate general representation of the terrain
slope and curvature. Regarding the cost of an accurate DEM and considering the
topology of the area of interest oﬀering the best conditions for generating SRTM
DEM with optimal accuracy, the SRTM open source product with application of
post-processing methods for noise removal seems to represent the best choice for
generating the terrain model.
4.1.2
Processing of the DEM data
The SRTM dataset can be processed by ﬁltering methods in order to eﬃciently re-
move the noise (Falorni et al., 2005; Gallant, 2011). A large variety of computed
methods for reducing DEM noise can be found in the literature. They result in
smoothing the raw surface by removing extreme values although ﬁltering has to
be chosen regarding the desired scale level and sharpness required. For instance,
cliﬀs are features that can be particularly aﬀected by smoothing processes. How-
ever, considering the low relief topography of the area, ﬁltering is expected to be
well adapted by accurately removing the noise as real sharp artefacts are rare in
the area of interest (Falorni et al., 2005; Gallant, 2011). Filtering representing a
simpliﬁcation in the terrain surface also result in a computational requirements
advantage for the hydrogeological model as the later is developed using the DEM.
The area size of the treated DEM was extended to 254 km2 in order to cover
all locations at which water measurements were performed. All the geographic
data were projected in the UTM zone 30N coordinate system. Note that this
projection is used throughout the present study in order to facilitate dimensional
representation of the area. The presence of voids, speckling and noise are initially
revealed by visual inspection of the 3D surface, the terrain slope and contour
lines generated from the SRTM raw data using the softwares ArcGIS and Matlab
==PAGE:31==
4.1 Digital elevation model
21
(Falorni et al., 2005). In order to minimize the computational power requirements
for the treatment and the manipulation of the elevation data between the various
software (i.e. importation of the DEM into Matlab and GMS MODFLOW), the
number of raster cells is reduced by aggregation into new cells with 60 m side
size using the software ArcGIS. The aggregation is performed by attributing to
new cells the average elevation values of the 4 corresponding cells with 30 m side
as recommended by Kuniansky et al. (2009).
Figure 4.1: Part of the raw SRTM DEM with aggregated cells of the study area
and with z magniﬁcation set to 10 for better visualization. Scalebar in [m].
DEM perceived as a 2-D signal can be ﬁltered by applying wavelets which
are derived from the ﬁeld of signal processing (Falorni et al., 2005; Kalbermatten
et al., 2011). The elevations at the centroïd of the generated cells were sampled
and imported to the Matlab software in order to perform the SRTM DEM noise
reduction using the Wavelet Toolbox utility (Falorni et al., 2005). As suggested
by Falorni et al. (2005), a 5.5 biorthogonal ﬁlter was applied although the level of
decomposition was set to 2 regarding the DEM quality required. For each level
of decomposition a speciﬁc threshold adapted to the sharpness of the ﬁgure was
calculated and applied.
The main objective of the smoothing process was deﬁned as to obtained a
simpliﬁed smoothed representation of the terrain with a general low slope oriented
towards the Mouhoun river. Considering that no ground control points or DEM
==PAGE:32==
22
4 Methods
with better accuracy were available for the considered area, diﬀerent assessments
for the evaluation of the DEM quality were performed:
• A qualitative assessment was made by visually evaluating the treated DEM
surface as well as the elevation and slope contour plots.
• The distribution of the elevations of the raw SRTM data should be optimally
preserved in order to avoid a loss of information. Therefore, the maximum,
minimum, average, median elevations as well as standard deviations and
skewness of the raw and denoised DEM were compared. The percentage of
elevations within 10 m range for both DEMs were also plot in a comparison
histogram.
• In order to quantify the change of elevations, the mean absolute diﬀerence
(MAD), the root mean square diﬀerence (RMSD) ( Equations 4.1 and 4.2)
as well as the minimum and maximum diﬀerences were calculated between
elevations of the raw and treated DEMs. This calculation was performed
for a) the entire range of the raw DEM elevations b) for raw DEM eleva-
tions between the 2.5-th and 97.5-th percentiles and c) for the raw DEM
elevations between the lower and upper quartiles.

4.2
Measurements and sampling
4.2.1
Methodology of ﬁeld work and objectives
Field work was performed during a total of 8 days between the 21st of April and
the 16th of May 2015 close to the end of the dry season. In order to conduct
rigorous measurements and analysis a simple sampling plan was developed.
The localization of the wells which were not identiﬁed during the past analysis,
was achieved by obtaining their coordinates from the Direction Générale des
==PAGE:33==
4.2 Measurements and sampling
23
Ressources en Eau (DGRE, 2014, personal communication) and by beneﬁting
from the help of the inhabitants and the local authorities. Locations and IDs of
the wells used for ﬁeld work are shown in Figure 4.2.
(a) Boreholes
(b) Dug wells
Figure 4.2: IDs and locations of the wells used for ﬁeld work except for A-83
which was only used during the previous sampling campaigns1.
.
Priority was set on sampling the wells that were not considered during the
previous campaign in order to complete the database and to obtain a better
knowledge about the distribution of arsenic contamination over the study area
regarding the water composition and the geology. In parallel, an assessment of
the ﬁeld hydrogeology (i.e. type of aquifer, groundwater ﬂow direction, deﬁni-
tion of the watershed) was performed by measuring groundwater levels and well
1Boreholes with IDs PO-1 to PO-12 and A-x were already sampled in February 2015
==PAGE:34==
24
4 Methods
dimensions in order to develop an hydrogeological model.
Secondary objective was set on carrying out groundwater sampling and anal-
ysis at multilevel depths in order to study the vertical proﬁle of the groundwater
composition and change in the arsenic concentration. Indeed, the fractured/ﬁs-
sured aquifer is likely to lead to stratiﬁed groundwater ﬂow and, therefore, to
signiﬁcant geochemical diﬀerences. This type of analysis was not performed in
previous studies concerning arsenic occurrence in African hard rocks aquifers (e.g.
COWI (2005) and Smedley et al. (2007)).
4.2.2
Positioning
The positions of wells, informal gold mining, tailings, abandoned quarries and
springs were systematically recorded using a GPS Garmin Etrex 10. This device
possess a horizontal accuracy of 3 m (read on the GPS) which is satisfying re-
garding the study scale and the 60 m horizontal accuracy of the treated DEM.
The position of the river and quarries were digitized from satellite imagery and
veriﬁed using ﬁeld GPS measurement.
4.2.3
Groundwater and surface water levels
Water level measurements in wells were performed among 27 boreholes and 10 dug
wells. The presence of electrical pumping representing signiﬁcant level of ground-
water abstraction was not found in the area. However, sporadic hand-pumping is
performed along the day for domestic needs. Thus, it was not possible to prevent
water abstraction before performing the measurements although hand-pumping
is not expected to signiﬁcantly aﬀect groundwater level. In fact, the ﬂow rate of
such pump is relatively limited (less than 1 m3/h) regarding the speciﬁc yield of
the boreholes ([0.64 ; 14] ; median: 6.2 m3/h). This was also veriﬁed by mea-
suring constant groundwater level while pumping for few minutes. Some of the
water levels were also measured a second time in the early morning (i.e. 5 a.m)
prior to any pumping. IDs and characteristics of the wells used for this analysis
can be found in Appendix C.
The water levels were measured from the top of the casing using a water
level dipper with centimetre accuracy. According to Fetter (2003), all water level
measurements should preferably be performed within a short time interval in or-
der to avoid water level changes. Regarding the constant dry seasonal weather
and the ﬁeld work period, this requirement is assumed to be respected and the
water level is not expected to change signiﬁcantly during the measurement period.
==PAGE:35==
4.2 Measurements and sampling
25
The total depths of the wells were measured from the top of the casings us-
ing the graduated tether cable of a point source bailer with meter accuracy. The
weight attached to the cable consisted in the bailer itself or a thinner item such as
a spanner in order to minimize groundwater mixing when performing multilevel
sampling. This measurement is considered accurate considering the uncertainty
on the real depth of the borehole due to sedimentary deposit.
The approximate surface water elevations at the two main quarries and of the
Mouhoun river were qualitatively determined through observations made in the
ﬁeld and by using the DEM:
• The water surface of the Mouhoun river was assumed to be constant over
the study area as its slope is relatively low. Its elevation was determined
using the DEM elevations as the surface water is almost equal to the banks
elevation (Figure D.3d)
• The water elevation of each quarry read on the raw DEM among cells
centroïds was highly variable. This could be due to the shadow of the cliﬀs
resulting in low light emission or to speckling due to light reﬂection on water
bodies (Falorni et al., 2005). Therefore, their elevations were qualitatively
determined using a) diﬀerences in GPS measurements (i.e. by measuring
elevations at the surface water body and at a ”ground control point” within
a short amount of time and by using the DEM to vertically reference the
ground control point), b) the range of raw DEM elevations of the surface
water, c) height measurement performed at the cliﬀof quarry B2 and by
vertically referencing the location of measurement using the DEM, d) by
visual estimate of the depth of the quarries.
Quarries B1 and B2 were
assumed to have equal surface water elevations regarding the relatively
short distance separating them and as they are linked by ﬂooded mining
tunnels.
4.2.4
Water chemistry ﬁeld measurements, sampling and
analysis
Two distinct types of sampling and measurements were performed in order to
achieve distinct analysis on the average water composition and on the change in
groundwater composition with depth.
The average composition of the water was determined by sampling the water
in the 3 quarries, in 3 shallow dug wells using a grab sampler (i.e. bailer) and in
18 boreholes using the integrated hand-pumping systems. In the last case, a ﬂow
==PAGE:36==
26
4 Methods
through cell was connected to the integrated hand-pump system in order to min-
imize the contact with the atmosphere. A multi-parameter meter connected to
probes measuring O2, pH, Eh, EC, T was used in order to visualize stabilization
of the parameters while applying constant pumping and to insure representative
sampling of the groundwater (Figure B.2). Once these parameters were relatively
stable, groundwater was sampled.
Multilevel sampling was performed among 5 boreholes (PO-1, PO-6, PO-7,
PO-10, PO-32) found contaminated by arsenic and/or located along the same
W-E line (i.e. assumed to be the approximate groundwater ﬂow direction) in
order to assess the fate and kinetics of arsenic transport in groundwater. A point
source bailer1 was used for this application.
The target depth intervals were
speciﬁcally deﬁned for each well regarding the water level, total depth and screen
interval. However, as the later information was not available for the majority
of the boreholes at the time of measurements, the target depths were arbitrary
deﬁned in order to cover the entire water column. The sampling points were also
suﬃciently spaced (i.e. minimum intervals of 10 m) in order to minimize water
mixing. When retrieving the bailer, O2, pH, Eh, EC, T were measured and the
turbidity of the groundwater was checked before sampling.
For both the average composition and the multilevel analysis, samples were
taken and conserved using three methods :
• in 30 mL polyethylene (PE) bottles: groundwater was ﬁltered using 0.45
µm pore size membrane for particulates removal, together with a disposable
anion exchange cartridge in order to selectively remove As(V) from the
sample by speciation of the inorganic arsenic species III and V. This allows
the measurement of As(III) concentration only. As the rate of oxidation
of As(III) to As(V) is very slow (Smedley et al., 1996), this method is
expected to be accurate. The sample was acidiﬁed to pH < 2 using nitric
acid HNO3. This method is consistent with the methodology outlined in
Kim et al. (2012).
• in 30 mL PE bottles: groundwater was ﬁltered using 0.45 µm pore size
membrane and syringe and the sample was acidiﬁed to pH < 2 using HNO3.
This allow the measurement of major cations (Na, K, Ca, Mg) and other
trace elements in particular the concentration of total arsenic AsT and total
iron concentration FeT .
1The Solinst Model 429 made of stainless steel, about 1.2 meter long and with a capacity
of 2 L
==PAGE:37==
4.3 Water chemistry and statistical analysis
27
• in 125 mL PE bottles: groundwater was kept non acidiﬁed and non ﬁltered
in order to measure the concentration of anions and the total inorganic
carbon (TIC). As the pH was found in the range of 6 to 7, TIC is mostly
present as HCO−
3 and can therefore be assumed to represent the alkalinity
(Appelo and Postma, 2010).
The samples were kept refrigerated until measurements (ca. 3 weeks). The
latter were all performed at EAWAG. Concentrations of anions (i.e. Cl−, SO2−
4 ,
F−, Br−, NO−
3 , PO3−
4 ) were measured with ion chromatography (IC) while ma-
jor cations (Na, K, Ca, Mg) and other trace elements (Fe, Si, B, Ba, Li, La, Ce
Sr, Al, Cu, Mn, Zn, Au, As, Be, Sb, Sc, Cd, Co, Cr, Mo, Pb, Rb, Sr, Tl, U, V,
W) were determined by inductively-coupled plasma mass spectrometer (ICP-MS).
Total inorganic carbon (TIC) was measured by acidifying the solution trans-
forming the inorganic carbon into CO2 which was subsequently measured. All
inorganic carbon was assumed to be present as HCO−
3 according to the circum-
neutral property of the water (Appelo and Postma, 2010). Thus, HCO−
3 mg/L
was calculated by dividing TIC [mg C/L] with the molar mass of carbon (i.e. 12
g/mol) and multiplying by the molar mass of HCO−
3 (61 g/mol).
Dissolved organic carbon (DOC) is measured by oxidizing all soluble mat-
ter to CO2 which is then measured (Appelo and Postma, 2010). As(III) was
measured by ICP-MS in a similar manner as the total concentration of arsenic
using the speciated sample. The concentration of As(V) was then determined by
subtracting As(III) from As(T ).
4.3
Water chemistry and statistical analysis
The ground and surface water geochemical analysis was performed using the re-
cent measurements (April-May 2015) and data collected during the February 2015
sampling campaign. Regarding the latter measurements, O2 and Eh parameters
were not used in this analysis as ﬂow through cell was not used.

Measurements accuracy

(E.B.) using the concentrations of major ions Na+, K+, Mg2+, Ca2+, Cl−, HCO−
3 ,

4.3.2
Physico-chemical parameters, major ion chemistry and

The major characteristics of the ground and surface water are determined by pro-

by investigating correlations between ECs and major ions. HCO−
3 concentrations
were also plot versus the borehole piezometric levels and depths in order to deter-
mine patterns between the groundwater composition and the residence time of
the groundwater . The total dissolved solids (TDS) in mg/L were calculated by
multiplying ECs by 0.65 (Fitts, 2002). Composition of the waters from boreholes,
dug wells and basins were compared.
In order to visualize the minerals saturation indexes (S.I.) for the boreholes
samples, speciation calculations using the software PHREEQC were performed.
For this application, physico-chemical parameters (pH, T) and major species

and its real value was calculated from the species present in solution.
In order to investigate change in water composition when groundwater ﬂows
into the basin, a simple batch experiment was performed using PHREEQC by
simulating equilibrium between representative groundwater samples (i.e. ground-
water in boreholes located close to the quarries) and the atmosphere. The atmo-
sphere was deﬁned by entering the activity corresponding to the partial pressure
of CO2 (i.e. PCO2 = log(10−3.5)1 (Appelo and Postma, 2010)) and the activity
corresponding to the partial pressure of O2 (i.e. PO2=-0.68).
