29
The concentrations of the species were also compared with the WHO guide-
lines. As discussed in Chapter 2, As release is likely to be due to the dissolution
of sulphide minerals which also generates Fe and SO2−
4 . Therefore, a speciﬁc
analysis is performed on the occurrence of Fe and SO2−
4
with respect to Eh and
pH.
4.3.3
Arsenic
The predominant arsenic specie (i.e. As(III) or As(V)) was determined by com-
paring the redox conditions and the As redox diagram and by visualizing the
measured As(III) and AsT .
Particular attention was brought on correlations
between As and species related to ore bodies (i.e. W, Mo, Sb), to sulphide min-
eralization (i.e. SO2−
4 , Fe) and to reductive dissolution of As-bearing oxides (i.e.
Mn, Fe) (COWI, 2005; Smedley et al., 2007; Verplanck et al., 2008). Relation-
ships between As and physico-chemical parameters (i.e. O2 and pH) were also
investigated.
The spatial distribution of arsenic was studied by plotting As concentrations
and discussed with respect to the identiﬁed potential sources and computed ﬂow
paths whereas the vertical distribution was discussed regarding the depths of the
contaminated boreholes and the diﬀerences in groundwater chemistry between
shallow dug wells and boreholes.
4.4
Boreholes technical data collection and
management
As no open source database containing technical details of boreholes (i.e. bore-
hole lithologs, total depths and screen intervals) were available for the study area,
informations were collected from several organizations and actors working in the
management of water resources as, for instance, DGRE (2015), WaterAid (2015),
personal communication.
Similarly as reported by Courtois et al. (2010), the main diﬃculty in this
study lay in the low amount of data available as well as their poor accuracy. For
instance, over a total of 16 lithologs found for the study area, 15 provided only
information about the total thickness of the weathered overburden. Informations
such as coordinates, screen intervals, total depths of the boreholes were also often
missing. Moreover, geological descriptions found in lithologs should be used with
==PAGE:40==
30
4 Methods
caution as this information often presents lack of accuracy.
By combining the collected borehole informations and establishing compari-
son with the measurements and observations made on the ﬁeld (i.e. total depths,
locations, type of pump, year of realisation), a database containing the approxi-
mate technical characteristics of boreholes was created (see Appendix C).
29 detailed borehole lithologs found for a larger area extent than the study
site (Table C.5) as well as the literature were also used to determine general
characteristics of the geological horizons and boreholes. This analysis resulted in
the following observations and assumptions:
• Boreholes are usually drilled down to the fresh basement in order to opti-
mize the collection of groundwater by crossing a maximal number of frac-
tures with high productivity. Therefore, using the thickness of the alterite
and the total depth of the corresponding borehole, the approximate thick-
ness of the ﬁssured layer can be determined at the considered location.
However, some boreholes and, in particular, the ones of higher depths, can
be drilled about 10 meters across the fresh basement or zone with a low
density of ﬁssures.
• According to Ahmed et al. (2008), Dewandel et al. (2006), Koïta et al.
(2013a), and Sharp (2014), the isalterite thickness is lower than the one
of the alloterite although this could not be conﬁrmed from the analysis of
the various lithologs. Indeed, the saprolite thickness highly varies within
short distances and none of these horizons was found generally thicker than
another. This could be a consequence of the alternation between the weath-
ering and erosion cycles with erosion partly impacting this layer resulting
in a multiphase weathering proﬁle as suggested by Ahmed et al. (2008) and
Dewandel et al. (2006).
• The lateritic cuirasse has a thickness in the range [2 ; 18] m with a median
value of 4 m showing the relatively low occurrence of ferricrete scarps (i.e.
highest thickness). This is in accordance with Bamba et al. (2002), Kote
et al. (2003), and Smedley et al. (2007) who reported that this horizons is
usually in the range [1 ; 5] m. According to the relative ﬂat topography of
the terrain, apart from the hills located in the east part of the study area,
the ferricrete thickness is expected to be in this range.
• The boreholes are exclusively screened between the base of the saprolite
and their total depths. The screen top position varies whereas the bottom
of the screen usually corresponds to the bottom of the borehole. It can
==PAGE:41==
4.5 Hydrogeological assessment: aquifer characteristics and groundwater ﬂow direction
31
also be divided into several intervals which likely correspond to conductive
ﬁssures parts.
4.5
Hydrogeological assessment: aquifer
characteristics and groundwater ﬂow direction
Ground water heads were obtained by subtracting the measured water levels from
the corresponding ground elevations obtained from the generated DEM. Thus, all
the water level data were managed referring to the Geoid datum. As no intensive
pumping was observed in the study area, all wells were considered of interest for
the hydrogeological assessment although water levels observed to change were
disregarded from the analysis. Considering the relative vertical accuracy of the
DEM, the calculated heads were considered whether realistic or not regarding
the expected general water ﬂow direction (i.e. towards the Mouhoun river).
First, the type of aquifer (i.e. unconﬁned, conﬁned) was assessed by compar-
ing the measured water levels with respect to the topography. A potentiometric
surface map (i.e. ﬂow net) was developed in order to estimate the groundwater
ﬂow direction by obtaining constant heads line (i.e. equipotential lines). How-
ever, such approach is only valid if the geological media can be assumed isotropic,
if the considered wells are screened in the same aquifer and if the groundwater
ﬂow is predominantly horizontal (Fetter, 2003; Fitts, 2002). Despite the presence
of local heterogeneity due to ﬁssures/fractures, the aquifer was considered as a
continuum equivalent porous medium on the scale of the study in order to obtain
an approximate direction of the groundwater ﬂow. Indeed, as discussed above
a relatively good conductivity between the ﬁssures is expected. Moreover, only
the boreholes were considered for this application as they are screened across the
ﬁssured layer whereas the dug wells receive water from the weathered overburden
and as the ﬁssured zone plays the major role for groundwater transmissivity. The
existence of horizontal ﬂow was assessed by comparing the range of piezometric
heads with the size of the study area.
As the aquifer thickness is expected to be low (i.e. less than 100 m) and as
the dug wells and boreholes are strictly screened respectively in the saprolite and
in the ﬁssured zone, comparing the piezometric levels measured at boreholes and
at dug wells for close locations would allow to determine the presence of a unique
or distinct aquifers.
==PAGE:42==
32
4 Methods
4.6
Localization of the potential sources of arsenic
4.6.1
Quartz veins
In West Africa, gold mineralization has been for long associated with the locations
of quartz veins and with the sulphide/arsenide minerals deposition, pyrite and
arsenopyrite being the dominant ore minerals (Béziat et al., 2008; Oberthür et al.,
1997). Conversely, the detection of arsenic is often considered as a good indica-
tor for the presence of gold. The main gold mineralization of the Poura area is
particularly associated with mesothermal quartz veins hosting rare polymetallic
sulphides and controlled by shear structures (Bamba, 1996; Bourges et al., 1998;
Béziat et al., 2008; Sanogo and Prost, 1993). As discussed above, oxidation and
dissolution of these sulphide minerals are expected to be the main source of As
in groundwater. Thus, by locating the quartz veins, the potential point sources
of arsenic can be determined.
Major gold deposits can be identiﬁed and located from the ancient mining
activities and from the descriptions and maps made by Bamba (1996), Gueye
(1984), Kote et al. (2003), and Sanogo and Prost (1993). Three principal gold
lodes, which are found close to each others, are usually identiﬁed as: the ”Plaine”,
”Ouest” and ”Montagne” deposits (Béziat et al., 2008; Gueye, 1984; Roy, 2000;
Sanogo and Prost, 1993). The former was the most exploited during industrial
mining and its location corresponds to the two abandoned quarries in the vicinity
of Poura-mine. The ”Ouest” and ”Montagne” deposits are found in the southern
extension of the Plaine deposit (Figure A.1). These major gold lodes are com-
posed of quasi-continuous steeply-dipping quartz veins (Béziat et al., 2008) with
depth of at least 400 m (Roy, 2000). It is therefore assumed that the quartz veins
vertically cross the entire hard rock aquifer. The main lodes found in the Poura
area are also narrow, having lateral dimensions that do not exceed the tenth of
meter. Some authors have reported approximate dimensions: the operated lode
ﬁlon ”Plaine” has a total strike length of about 2000 m and a width ranging from
0.5 to 8 m with an average of 2 m (Kote et al., 2003; Roy, 2000). ”Filon Ouest”
and Filon Montagne” have lengths of 250 m and 300 m and widths in the range
[0.8 ; 3], [0.8 ; 6] m respectively (Gueye, 1984).
Figure 3.4 shows that numerous other minor quartz veins are likely to be dis-
tributed over the study area as well as they can occur in all lithologies (Béziat
et al., 2008). However, the dimensions of the veins shown in this Figure don’t
seem realistic (i.e. widths of about 150 m) and it might only indicate zones with
potential quartz mineralization. Bamba (1996) indicated with a better accuracy
the position of quartz veins showing on the surface on a geomorphological map
==PAGE:43==
4.6 Localization of the potential sources of arsenic
33
(Figure A.2). Therefore, these quartz veins positions were digitized and consid-
ered as potential As point sources. Descriptions of Gueye (1984) and Sanogo and
Prost (1993) were also used to more accurately deﬁne the positions of the main
lodes and borehole lithologs were studied to reveal wells crossing quartz veins.
Moreover, the oxidation of sulphide minerals can be assumed to occur over the
entire saturated zone as the groundwater is typically oxic in this type of aquifer
(Roy, 2000; Smedley et al., 2007).
Localization of informal mining activity in the ﬁeld was also used as an in-
dicator to locate potential gold mineralization. Indeed, according to Kote et al.
(2003), informal gold mining occurs at locations of the veins associated with gold-
free deposit and oxidized sulphide minerals. Hand dug mining is performed using
pits and tunnels across the altered horizons and reaching depth of 20-30 m cor-
responding to the hydrostatic groundwater level.
4.6.2
Other potential sources
Arsenic could also be found in various host rocks into As-bearing disseminated
sulphide mineralization (Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002). For instance, the Tark-
waïan rocks in the vicinity of Poura can locally show a disseminated gold mineral-
ization together with sulphide minerals (Kote et al., 2003; Oberthür et al., 1997).
Large zones of the andesite were also altered by hydrothermal ﬂuids (Figure 3.4).
This has led to disseminated sulphides (especially pyrite) deposits within the
schistosity without being associated with large quartz veins Kote et al. (2003).
Thus, these large zones might constitute diﬀuse As sources
Evidences for direct and indirect pollution by mining activities were also col-
lected by observations of the ongoing informal mining and by locating remnants
of the past industrial activities. Indeed, according to Kagambega et al. (2014b),
mine wastes were observed to be a signiﬁcant source of arsenic contamination
in the surroundings of Poura. Thus, their location was assessed in the ﬁeld and
compared with the As distribution.
4.6.3
Model-based interpretation
The likelihood of the As sources was assessed by the development of an hydroge-
ological model in order to observe the potential lateral transport of arsenic and
by comparing these results with the distribution of measured As concentrations.
The latter were also plotted on a geological map.
==PAGE:44==
34
4 Methods
4.7
Hydrogeological model
A static hydrogeological model was developed to assess the sources and the spatial
distribution of arsenic by simulating its transport through groundwater regard-
ing the location of the identiﬁed quartz veins and the distribution of arsenic
concentrations in groundwater.
4.7.1
Model code
To simulate the groundwater ﬂow system and the interaction between groundwa-
ter and surface water, the MODFLOW-2000 code together with the Groundwater
Modeling System (GMS) graphical user interface (GUI) were chosen.
MOD-
FLOW is a three dimensional, ﬁnite diﬀerence, block centered model which has
been developed since 1984 by the U.S Geological Survey. It solves the three di-
mensional groundwater ﬂow equation (Equation 4.4) designed to simulate aquifer
systems assuming constant density and dynamic viscosity. The solver applies a
ﬁnite-diﬀerence method, using the law of mass balance and Darcy’s Law, to a
discretized aquifer domain in order to describe the ﬂow from cell to cell.
∂
∂x · (Kxx
∂h
∂x) + ∂
∂y · (Kyy
∂h
∂y ) + ∂
∂z · (Kzz
∂h
∂z ) −W = SS · ∂h
∂t
(4.4)
where:
• Kxx, Kyy, Kzz are the hydraulic conductivity along the x, y and z coordi-
nate axes, which are assumed to be parallel to the major axes of hydraulic
conductivity [m/day]
• h is potentiometric head [m]
• W is the volumetric ﬂux per unit volume representing the sources and/or
sinks of water [1/day]
• Ss is the speciﬁc storage [1/m]
• t is the time [day]
The layer property ﬂow (LPF) package is used as it calculates conductance
coeﬃcients at the node of each model cell and allows the deﬁnition of vertical/hor-
izontal anisotropy in the hydraulic conductivity.
==PAGE:45==
4.7 Hydrogeological model
35
4.7.2
Conceptual model
4.7.2.1
Layers deﬁnition
As discussed in Sections 3.3 and 3.4, the recent research results having included
the underlying ﬁssured layer as part of the weathering proﬁle have resulted in a
better conceptualization of hard rock aquifers (Ahmed et al., 2008; Sharp, 2014).
Indeed, the alteritic horizons and the ﬁssured bedrock together are assumed to
form a composite stratiform aquifer with variable hydrogeological properties. A
conceptual model of the aquifer at catchment scale was developed using the GMS
interface by creating a simpliﬁed 3D geological model over which hydrogeological
parameters were assigned.
Due to the uncertainties remaining on the geology discussed in Section 4.4,
the geological model was kept to a simple complexity level by only considering the
two main stratiform weathered layers (i.e. the saprolite and the ﬁssured layer)
as well as the fresh basement (Figure 4.3). This simpliﬁcation also presented the
advantage to minimize the number of cells and parameters leading to a decrease
in the computational power requirements. The geological model was obtained by
interpolation of 16 simpliﬁed petrological proﬁles deﬁned at locations where the
ﬁssured layer thickness could be determined and by using the DEM as the upper
surface.
The saprolite layer was deﬁned as an isotropic porous medium (Ahmed et al.,
2008; Compaore et al., 1997; Wright and Burgess, 1992) and assumed to cover
the bedrocks over the entire study area. The soil, ferricrete and mottled clay
horizons were disregarded and considered as part of the saprolite as expected to
be of low inﬂuence on the model output. In fact, saturated zones in the soil and
ferricrete are not expected during the time of the study and due to the relatively
similar clayey content of the weathered overburden horizons (Bamba et al., 2002),
their hydrodynamic properties are likely to be similar. The diﬀerence between
the alloterite and isalterite was disregarded as no clear distinction could be de-
ﬁned.
The productive weathered ﬁssured/fractured basement is represented as an
equivalent porous medium analogously to a similar model in Ahmed et al. (2008).
Its thickness was considered constant as discussed in Section 3.3. However, ac-
cording to the data collected, the weathered overburden thickness ([6 ; 50] ;
median: 23 m) and the borehole depths ([36 ; 89] ; median: 47 m) don’t linearly
vary between the boreholes. Substracting the alterite thickness from the total
depths of the corresponding boreholes to determine the ﬁssured layer thickness
gives a range of [21 ; 49] m. Thus, an approximate constant thickness of 30 m was
==PAGE:46==
36
4 Methods
calculated as the median of these diﬀerences. This result is in accordance with
Bamba et al. (2002) who observed, in the vicinity of the study area, a ﬁssured
layer thickness of 32.5 m. A distinct upper part of the bedrocks having an higher
density of ﬁssures as reported in the literature was not included in the model as
no information could be obtained about the vertical fractures distribution within
the study area and as lateral heterogeneity of ﬁssures is expected to play the
deterministic role in groundwater ﬂow.
Regarding the scale of the study and the scarcity of deep tectonic fractures,
the fresh unfractured basement was considered impervious which is in accordance
with Maréchal et al. (2004). The alluvium was also disregarded as it constitutes
a clayey superﬁcial layer not well developed, located close to the river boundary.
Figure 4.3:
Geological proﬁle considered over the model domain.
The ﬁs-
sured/fractured weathered bedrocks and the fresh basement have constant thick-
ness while the saprolite has a variable thickness.
==PAGE:47==
4.7 Hydrogeological model
37
4.7.2.2
Heterogeneity
Heterogeneities in the weathered bedrocks are particularly expected to play an
important role in controlling ﬂow and solute movement in the subsurface (Moeck
et al., 2015) considering that the transmissivity of the aquifer is primary assured
by the ﬁssures/fractures. This was modeled by attributing laterally variable hy-
draulic conductivities (K) to the model grid. Two distinct conceptual approaches
were used to model the heterogeneity:
• According to Ahmed et al. (2008), for numerical groundwater ﬂow mod-
elling and transport processes in such environments, the aquifer should be
divided and discretized into a number of subvolumes having uniform prop-
erties. As the latter couldn’t be measured in the ﬁeld, the aquifer domain
was divided manually according to the diﬀerent entities of the lithology
shown on the geological map (Doherty, 2003; Koïta et al., 2013b). This di-
vision was applied similarly on the saprolite and the ﬁssured layers as their
properties are closely related to the type of basement rocks (Ahmed et al.,
2008; Koïta et al., 2013b). Subareas were deﬁned for i) rhyolite and rhyo-
dacite, ii) andesitic tuﬀ, iii) andesite1, iv) Tarkwaian rocks, v) Quartzite,
vi) Volcano-sedimentary schist according to Figure 3.4. Faults appearing
on the geological map (Figure 3.4) were also included in the model by at-
tributing speciﬁc K to the corresponding cells of the ﬁssured layer. In fact,
according to Ahmed et al. (2008) and Sharp (2014) this major geological fea-
tures could have increased the weathering process in their vicinity resulting
in zones of enhanced hydraulic conductivities.
• Spatial variation of hydraulic properties can also be modeled through an
inverse modeling approach known as pilot points. This method consists
in attributing speciﬁc hydraulic conductivity values to points distributed
over the study area. K contained in the pilot points are assigned to the
grid elements through spatial interpolation and their values are determined
through calibration (Doherty, 2003; Moeck et al., 2015).
191 regularly
distributed pilot points spaced by about 500 m were deﬁned over the model
domain (Figure 4.4). This number was chosen as a reasonable compromise
between eﬃcient model-run time, the low amount of observed data and the
recommendations found in the literature. Indeed, Doherty (2003), Doherty
et al. (2010a), and Moeck et al. (2015) advised to use a high number of
pilot points in the case of a complex heterogeneous system in order to
minimize artifacts associated with oversimpliﬁcation.
For instance, this
aims to avoid the presence of ”bulls eyes” (i.e. artifacts of extreme hydraulic
conductivities) in the calibrated model and to obtain a smoothed spatial
1the andesite included also the hydrothermalized andesite
==PAGE:48==
38
4 Methods
repartition of the hydraulic properties. Ks were spatially interpolated to the
model grid using the ordinary Kriging method based on a log variogram
of the exponential type (Doherty et al., 2010b) with a range of 1000 m
and a nugget of zero.
This means that each pilot point inﬂuences the
K distribution over a distance of 1 km from its location although it has
a much greater inﬂuence on K in its close vicinity. This variogram was
arbitrary designed in order to obtain a smoothed K distribution as it was
not possible to produce an experimental variogram due to the absence of K
measurements.
4.7.3
Model domain, sources and sinks
Figure 4.4: Model domain with locations of the 14 observed wells (black dots),
regularly distributed 191 pilot points (squares). Constant head boundaries are
shown by the solid lines. The dashed lines consist of no ﬂow boundaries.
The boundaries of the model correspond to the watershed delimitation de-
scribed in Section 5.2. The west boundary coincides with the Mouhoun river and
was deﬁned as constant head of 240 m. The remaining boundaries (i.e. north,
==PAGE:49==
4.7 Hydrogeological model
39
south and east) of the model were set at the expected limit of the watershed cor-
responding to groundwater divides and were represented as no-ﬂow boundaries.
As no recharge is expected during the study period and as the groundwater
ﬂows towards the Mouhoun river, a constant head boundary corresponding to an
equipotential line of 270 m was deﬁned on the east side of the model domain in
order to create a groundwater inﬂow.
The three quarries representing signiﬁcant water surface features were deﬁned
as constant speciﬁed head in the model.
4.7.4
Grid deﬁnition
Each layer was divided into 45545 square meshes with 38 - m size over the model
domain. This was chosen as a reasonable compromise between the minimization
of the computational power requirement (i.e. by limiting this number to 100,000)
and the representation of the ﬁssured layer heterogeneity on the catchment scale.
The number and size of cells in the vertical direction correspond to the deﬁned
geological layers.
4.7.5
Model aquifer characteristics
No hydraulic tests could be carried out on the ﬁeld in order to deﬁne the hydro-
dynamic properties of the aquifer. Moreover, as the developed model is steady
state, hydraulic conductivity (K) is the only parameter having an inﬂuence on the
spatial head repartition and dynamic parameters such as storativity were disre-
garded. Therefore, values from the literature were used to deﬁne the calibration
range of Ks assigned to each of the conceptual layers. Table 4.1 presents ranges
of conductivity for the saprolite and the ﬁssured layer estimated from various
tests and lithology.
Many hydrodynamic data from various ﬁeld tests are available for the sapro-
lite layer. As shown in Table 4.1, its K is highly variable having a total range of
[2E-10 ; 1E-5] m/s which is due to the variance in the kaolinite content (Wright
and Burgess, 1992). As a few meters of groundwater was measured in the shallow
dug wells crossing this layer, the latter is expected to exhibit a suﬃcient trans-
missivity to recharge those wells. Thus, lowest value of K, as proposed by Koïta
et al. (2013a), don’t seem realistic for the considered area. The lower bound was
therefore arbitrary ﬁxed to 1E-8 m/s to account for very low conductivity while
the upper bound was deﬁned to 1E-5 m/s which is of same order of magnitude
==PAGE:50==

of the saprolite and the ﬁssured layer for hard rock aquifers.

as the highest value of conductivity found in the literature.
The K of the fractured/ﬁssured layer primary depends on the fracture density
and geometry, the rock matrix being impervious (Ahmed et al., 2008). Very low
Ks as reported by Howard et al. (1992) and Maréchal et al. (2004) are likely to
correspond to the aquifer characteristics in the impervious rock matrix. How-
ever, the ﬁssured layer was considered vertically homogeneous and no evidence
of impervious weathered bedrocks was observed in the ﬁeld as no wells were dry.
Permeability was therefore allowed at all location considering that conductivity
in the weathered bedrocks is expected to be higher than in the saprolite. Thus,
the lower bound of this layer was ﬁxed to 10E-7 m/s in order to be higher than
the lower bound of the saprolite layer. The upper bound was deﬁned equal to
5E-4 m/s as the highest K found in the literature (Table 4.1). This large range
of Ks enables realistic ﬂexibility for the calibration of the ﬁssured layer in order
to simulate the signiﬁcant heterogeneity of the ﬁssures/fractures. Note also that
these K values are statistical values accounting for the bedrock matrix and the
ﬁssures/fractures. It is therefore expected that the real K of a single fracture is
much higher than this statistical value.
Regarding the structural geology of the area and the general orientation North-
South of the vertical schistosity and faults, an anisotropy in the hydraulic con-
ductivity might be expected. As discussed above, many authors have also argued
that the upper part of the weathered bedrocks is aﬀected by a dense horizontal
ﬁssuring while the ﬁssures density decreases with depth (Courtois et al., 2010;
==PAGE:51==
4.7 Hydrogeological model
41
Dewandel et al., 2006, 2011; Maréchal et al., 2004; Wyns et al., 1999). Such
anisotropy was particularly observed by Dewandel et al. (2011) who found a ra-
tio Kh/Kv up to 10 from pumping test. Therefore, a vertical anisotropy was
deﬁned in the ﬁssured layer. However, due to the uncertainty remaining on the
orientation of the ﬁssures in the present study the calibration range was ﬁxed to
[0.1 - 10]. The conductivity of the fresh basement was ﬁxed to 10−9 m/s in order
deﬁne it as impervious.
4.7.6
Model calibration
4.7.7
Calibration procedure
The model was calibrated for both conceptual approach using the automated
nonlinear parameter estimation software PEST so that the model output ﬁts the
measured hydraulic heads. The objective function to be minimized is deﬁned as
(Doherty, 2003):
Φm = (d −M(p)))tQ1(d −M(p))
(4.5)
where d is a vector of ﬁeld measurements (in our case, the observed heads), p
is the vector of parameters that are calibrated, M describes the model action on
the parameters to generate outputs similar to d, Q1 incorporates the weights as-
signed to the various observations according to their contribution in minimizing
Equation 4.5 for an optimal parameter set. The parameters optimised were the
diﬀerent hydraulic conductivities assigned to the saprolite and weathered ﬁssured
bedrocks as well as the anisotropic term deﬁned for the ﬁssured layer.
As the use of pilot points result in a number of parameters to be estimated
greatly exceeding the number of observations, a ”regularization objective func-
tion” (Equation 4.6) has to be added to Equation 4.5 in order to form a global
objective function (Equation 4.7) (Doherty, 2003). The regularization function
adds a number of additional mathematical constraints on the parameters variance
required to perform calibration by achieving parsimony and to ensure numerical
stability (Doherty, 2003).
Φr = (e −R(p)))tQ2(e −R(p))
(4.6)
where R is a ”regularization operator” acting on the parameters vector p
and e expresses some ”default system state”. Q2 contains the weights that are
assigned to the various ”regularization observations” contained in e. Diﬀerent
regularization techniques were used together:
==PAGE:52==
42
4 Methods
• The nonlinear homogeneous Tikhonov regularization scheme aims at min-
imizing the number of possible parameters set solution by adding an ho-
mogeneity constraint on the parameters (i.e. the values assigned to pilot
points that are close to each other should be similar) (Doherty, 2015).
• The preferred value Tikhonov regularization scheme allows to constrain the
parameter value near its initial value in order to avoid it to hit its bound
(Doherty, 2015).
• The subspace regularization technique Singular Value Decomposition (SVD)
limits the number of linear combinations of the estimated parameters based
on the information content of the data available for calibration. This re-
sults in reducing the parameter space by the consideration of parameter
combinations rather than parameters themselves (Doherty, 2015).
.
Φg = Φm + µΦr
(4.7)
where µ is a value numerically calculated by PEST along the calibration pro-
cess.
The SVD-Assist scheme was used in parallel to the regularization techniques
in order to reduce the number of estimated parameters and, thus, of model runs
by generating super-parameters.
4.7.7.1
Initial parameter values and observed data
As no speciﬁc information about the hydrodynamic properties of the considered
aquifer could be found, the initial parameter values were deﬁned as the geomet-
rical means of K range listed in Table 4.1 as well as the initial anisotropy was
deﬁned to one. This was decided in order to minimize the inﬂuence of the initial
value on the calibration.
Head data from 15 boreholes screened in the ﬁssured layer were used as steady
state head targets. As PO-8 and PO-9 are very close to each others and as PO-
8 exhibits a slightly higher head, PO-9 was disregarded in order to reduce the
weight of observations at this location.
Observed heads deﬁned in the model
can be found in Table E.2. The uncertainty range was ﬁx to ±1 m with 95%
conﬁdence interval assuming that the error was only generated by water levels
measurements.
==PAGE:53==
4.7 Hydrogeological model
43
4.7.8
Validation of the model
4.7.8.1
Model performance
The model performance and validation was evaluated through multi-performance
criteria as recommended by Bennett et al. (2013) and Moeck et al. (2015).
The global objective function value Φg was analyzed to evaluate the conver-
gence of the calibration. The bias, mean absolute error (MAE) and root mean
square error (RMSE) between observed and computed heads calculated by PEST
were used for direct value comparison between observed and computed heads.
Note that the MAE and RMSE are calculated similarly as in Equations 4.1 and
4.2 while bias is the average of the diﬀerences between observed and computed
heads. The diﬀerence in computed and observed heads were also checked individ-
ually in order to observe where the model performs the worse.
Considering the scarcity and the poor reliability of the data, a qualitative
assessment of the model output is performed. Scatter and residual plots were
ﬁrst used in order to reveal unmodeled behaviour. The realism of the hydraulic
conductivity distribution of the ﬁssured layer over the domain was then veriﬁed
visually by plotting K of the ﬁssured layer over the model domain.
For the speciﬁc qualitative assessment of the pilot point approach, the pres-
ence of eventual bull’s eyes (i.e. point artefacts exhibiting extreme values) in
the K distribution was checked. The range, geometrical means and geometric
standard deviation of K were also used to describe the distribution. Comparison
between the spatial hydraulic conductivity distribution and the geological entities
was also considered as a good indicator of the output likelihood.
4.7.8.2
Model uncertainty
Multiple sets of parameters might lead to similar minimizations of the objective
function through the existence of several local minima (Doherty et al., 2010a,b).
This is particularly the case here as important conceptual and spatial simpliﬁca-
tions were made and as low mathematical constraints on parameter estimation
through regularization were deﬁned.
The weak regularization primary results
from the absence of information about the hydrogeology and geology and the low
number of observed heads. The various sets of solution parameters can be de-
composed in two subsets: ”the calibration solution space” that can be estimated
uniquely and ”the calibration null-space” accounting for parameters or combi-
nations of parameters that cannot be estimated on the basis of the calibration
==PAGE:54==
44
4 Methods
dataset (Doherty et al., 2010a). Indeed, the null-space has a low sensitivity on the
model output and does not aﬀect the calibrated state. However, the existence of
diﬀerent K distribution leads to multiple possibilities of groundwater ﬂow paths.
It is therefore of particular importance to test the variance of model output while
maintaining the calibrated state in order to estimate the uncertainty on model
prediction as well as the likelihood of the spatial occurrence of arsenic.
The predictive uncertainty analysis of the model is performed through the
exploration of the null-space, known as the High-resolution Monte Carlo analysis,
in order to generate a probability distribution of the solution (Refsgaard et al.,
2012). By varying the initial values assigned to the pilot points and running
diﬀerent calibration process, various sets of optimized parameters are generated.
With the purpose to reduce the computational power requirements and simu-
lation time, only ten sets of initial parameters were generated. Random initial
parameter values for pilot points were generated using the RANDPAR (i.e. RAN-
Dom PARameter) utility supplied with PEST by specifying the range of hydraulic
conductivity of the ﬁssured layer deﬁned above.
Once the solutions were generated, the simulations presenting poor-calibration
performances were rejected for the predictive uncertainty analysis. The diﬀerent
K distributions of the converging solutions were qualitatively compared by plot-
ting them on surface contour maps. Maximum, minimum and geometric means of
Ks were also compared. The distribution of the K dispersion among the converg-
ing solutions was qualitatively assessed by calculating and plotting the geometric
standard deviations (GSD) of Ks for each cell considering all converging models
using Equation 4.8). Thus, zones with signiﬁcant variations in K were identiﬁed.
The mean, median, maximum and minimum values of these GSDs were also used
to quantitatively assess the K dispersion.
σg = exp
√∑n
i=1(ln Ki
µg )2
n
(4.8)
with n is the number of solutions presenting a good convergence, K is the hy-
draulic conductivity attributed to the considered cell, µg is the geometric mean
of Ks attributed to the considered cell among the converging solutions.
The variations in the saprolite Ks and of the ﬁssured layer anisotropy for
converging models were also assessed by observations of the range, geometric
mean (or mean in the case of the saprolite).
==PAGE:55==
4.7 Hydrogeological model
45
4.7.8.3
Sensitivity analysis
A sensitivity analysis was performed in order to complete the predictive uncer-
tainty analysis of the model.
In fact, uncertainty on the modeled hydraulic
conductivity distribution also arises from the presence of troublesome parame-
ters insensitive to the model or parameters that are highly correlated with others
(Doherty et al., 2010b). These insensitive parameters might lead to stable Ks
attributed to the model grid poorly representative of the reality as insensitive pa-
rameters are hold constant by PEST utilities during the calibration. Estimating
the sensitivity of the parameters therefore support the evaluation of the model
outcome likelihood by deﬁning zones where K is likely to be erroneous and where
more observations should be performed.
According to the large number of parameters (i.e. pilot points) and the non
linear relationship between parameters and output due to the mathematical con-
straints (i.e. synergistic eﬀect) between the parameters, the simple approach by
which one parameter at a time is varied to test the model output is not adapted.
Instead, the composite sensitivities of the parameters calculated with respect to
all observations (Doherty, 2015) were used to perform the sensitivity analysis.
All considered converging solutions were also used in this analysis. The sensitiv-
ities were ﬁrst normalized dividing their values by the sum of the sensitivities of
the respective simulations. The median and range of sensitivities of the saprolite
K and of the ﬁssured layer anisotropy were analyzed by comparison of the pilot
points median composite sensitivities of th pilot points. The latter were also plot
using graduated symbols in order to distinguish zones where parameters have
lower sensitivities.
4.7.9
Arsenic transport
Besides the uncertainty analysis of the groundwater ﬂow, the MODPATH utility
together with the MODFLOW models presenting a satisfying convergence were
used to generate forward several sets of potential ﬂow paths transporting As in
order to delineate zones of probable contamination. The sources of arsenic were
deﬁned at the cells of the ﬁssured layer corresponding to positions of the quartz
veins digitized from Bamba (1996). The choice of generating the particles sources
only at the ﬁssured layer results from that the groundwater transmissivity func-
tion is essentially insured by this horizon and that the boreholes were the only
wells to be found contaminated.
16 particles were generated at the center of
each cell. In a similar manner, particles were generated backwards from the wells
found contaminated in order to determine the sources locations. The various
==PAGE:56==
46
4 Methods
generated particles ﬂow paths were then plotted on maps together with the As
distribution measured among the boreholes in order to observe correlations be-
tween the quartz veins positions and contamination.
A simple average model was developed using the various converging model
outputs in order to compute the most probable case of ﬂow paths. This was
performed by the calculation of the geometric mean of Ks and of the median
value of the anisotropy predicted by the diﬀerent simulations. These parameters
were then re-allocated to the mesh. According to Refsgaard et al. (2012), this is
a very robust model prediction resulting in a larger, and more realistic estimate
of the prediction uncertainty than using a single model. All models considered
as presenting a satisfying convergence were equally weighted for the calculation.
Indeed, it is not because a model performs best against the observation data, that
it will similarly performed best for the prediction of K or As transport (Refsgaard
et al., 2012).
A speciﬁc study was performed on quartz veins that are supposed to lead
to a signiﬁcant pollution of groundwater. These veins were selected based on
potential As ﬂow paths appearing from their locations and crossing positions of
contaminated boreholes. The residence time of the groundwater from the veins
to the river was calculated and diﬀerence in As concentrations among the wells
locating in the considered ﬂow path were compared.

5.2
Piezometric map and aquifer characteristics
Figure 5.3 presents the distribution of measured heads over the study area. The
measured water level at borehole PO-37 was not counted in the analysis as a
change in the water level was observed during measurement.
The distribution of these piezometric levels conﬁrms that the groundwater
ﬂow is oriented towards the river. Moreover, the measured water levels are found
in a narrow range [5.95 ; 15.88] m regarding the total longitudinal distance (ca
5 km) and the range of terrain elevations at the boreholes [248 ; 282] m. It can
therefore be inferred that the position of the water table follows the general shape
of the topography. This is usually an intrinsic property of an unconﬁned aquifer
(Fetter, 2003). As another consequence of the low total variation of piezometric
heads regarding the total longitudinal distance, horizontal ﬂow is expected to
==PAGE:60==
50
5 Results
predominate in the aquifer.
Over the study area, the borehole yields are in the range [0.64 ; 14] with a
median of 6.2 m3/h (see Table C.4). No borehole was observed to be dry. This
shows that the hydraulic properties are relatively similar over the study area and
reinforce the hypothesis of a laterally unique aquifer entity over the study area.
(a) Boreholes and approximate ﬂow map
(b) Shallow dug wells
Figure 5.3: Piezometric heads distribution, watershed boundary and estimated
surface water elevation
Figure 5.3 shows that the water levels measured at dug wells and boreholes
close from each other are very similar. This suggests a good connectivity between
the ﬁssured layer and the weathered overburden and the existence of a vertically
unique aquifer which is in accordance with the description found for hard rock
aquifers in the literature (e.g. (Ahmed et al., 2008; Carrier et al., 2008; Sharp,
2014)). The range of piezometric heads [236 ; 270] m is also relatively narrow
regarding the longitudinal distance of the area (ca. 5 km) which suggests an
horizontal groundwater ﬂow.
The water table being closely related to the topography, the aquifer or sub-
aquifer domain of interest could be delineated from the analysis of the terrain ele-
vation. East of the study area, the hills delimiting the Mouhoun valley are likely
==PAGE:61==
5.3 Localization of arsenic sources
51
to represent a boundary where the groundwater divides. South, a groundwater di-
vide is also expected as measured piezometric decrease towards the Sambayourou
river. According to Figure D.2, watershed boundaries proposed by Kagambega et
al. (2014b) are in accordance with these observations while Ahmed et al. (2008)
stated that for this type of aquifer the groundwater watershed corresponds to
the topographical basin or sub-basin limits. Therefore, the boundaries given by
Kagambega et al. (2014b) were used to delimit the aquifer domain boundaries of
the study area (see Figure 5.3).
According to the properties of the aquifer cited above and using the distribu-
tion of heads measured at boreholes, a water table map of the aquifer is shown
in Figure 5.3. This provides an estimate of the general groundwater ﬂow direc-
tion over the entire area although the heterogeneity due to ﬁssures and fractures
are expected to play a major role in controlling the ﬂow direction locally. For
instance the head distribution between boreholes A-82 (267 m), PO-28 (265 m),
PO-21 (269 m) could suggest a local enhancement of the groundwater ﬂow along
a line equidistant from A-82 and PO-28 and passing by PO-21. In fact, this ﬁts
with the position of a major fault drawn on the geological map (see Figure 3.4).
In addition, the water contained in the quarries are expected to interact with the
groundwater as the surface water level was found to be less than the piezometric
heads measured in its vicinity, the quarries acting consequently as sink for the
groundwater ﬂowing from the East side.
5.3
Localization of arsenic sources
Figure 5.4 shows the locations of the identiﬁed features that are potential ar-
senic sources. Positions of the quartz veins showing on the surface digitized from
Bamba (1996) and acquired by GPS show that these entities are well distributed
over the area and mostly oriented NW-SE. The major lode (i.e. the Plaine de-
posit) is represented as the longest quartz vein at the center of the map. Its
position corresponds to the abandoned quarries (i.e. Basin 1 and Basin 2). Note
that the litholog of borehole PO-6 revealed the presence of a quartz vein within
the borehole proﬁle.
==PAGE:62==
52
5 Results
Figure 5.4: Map of the potential sources for As in groundwater. Locations of the
quartz veins with (1): digitized from Bamba (1996) and (2): located from GPS
measurement and borehole litholog. The size of the features was exaggerated for
better representation. The font corresponds to the map found in (Bamba, 1996).
Bologo, a village located about 4 km South West of Poura-Mine (See Figure
3.7) was the site of minor industrial mining operations (Kote et al., 2003) that
have resulted in the abandoned quarry B3 shown in Figure 5.4). The position of
two quartz veins located nearby this quarry matches with the locations of impor-
tant hand dug mining activities. Moreover, according, to Ouédraogo et al. (2003),
this corresponds to a network of small quartz veins of white and grey colours.
Contrary to the case of Bologo, observed hand-dug mining activity cannot be
all considered for locating quartz veins or signiﬁcant sulphide minerals deposit.
Indeed, some mining tunnels were observed without any evidence of activity and
might only be remnants of gold exploration as well as mining can only be super-
ﬁcial within the weathered overburden (i.e. 1 - 2 m deep). Moreover, tailings
washing for gold collection was also observed locally (see Figure B.5). This is
mostly performed at the surface water banks of the quarries.
Important mine tailings were also observed on the edges of the quarries B1 an
B2 (Kafando, 2012) and a 3000 m2 open-air landﬁll for cyanide-containing waste
is located about 1 km south from them. These wastes were generated during
the past industrial mining activities (i.e. 80’s and 90’s) and could constitute an
important load of heavily polluted eﬄuents through the percolation of rainwater
which might reach the surface or/and groundwater.

5.4 Groundwater chemistry
55
5.4.2.1
Boreholes
The groundwater from boreholes is circumneutral (pH ranging from 6.25 to 7.37)
and oxic/sub-oxic with O2 varying in the range [0.4 ; 6.26] mg/L with a median
of 1.2 mg/L and Eh in the range [69 ; 299] mV with a median of 260 mV. Thus,
groundwater can be mildly reducing. The TDS concentrations are moderate ([127
; 406] mg/L) conﬁrming that the groundwater is fresh, respecting the aesthetic
criteria of <500 mg/L and showing that the groundwater has undergone interac-
tions with the weathered rocks.
Figure 5.7: Most signiﬁcant correlations found between EC and major ions.
Figure 5.6 shows that the groundwater is of calcium carbonate or Ca-Mg-
Na-HCO3 types with high concentrations of silica ([7.61 ; 28.87], median: 19.95
mg/L) and from speciation calculations (shown in Appendix G), it appears that
the groundwater is saturated with calcite (CaCO3), dolomite (CaMg(CO3)2), and
quartz (SiO2). This is consistent with the correlations calculated between major
constituents and EC and plotted in Figure 5.7. The formation of bicarbonate
and the dissolution of calcium and magnesium minerals seems to primary con-
trol the major chemistry. The correlation coeﬃcients between Na and EC and
between Si and EC are lower with R-Na+ = 0.52 and R-Si = 0.44 respectively
while correlation with other major ions are not found (R-K+ = -0.09, R-Cl−=
0.13, R-SO2−
4
= 0.33).
The groundwater characteristics typically reﬂect the action of silicate weath-
ering from metasedimentary and volcanic rocks (e.g. biotite and calcic or epidotic
==PAGE:66==
56
5 Results
plagioclase is particularly found within the andesitic formations present over most
of the study area (Kote et al., 2003)) as described by Appelo and Postma (2010):
• Silicate weathering consumes protons (i.e. buﬀers the pH) leading to the for-
mation of bicarbonate. It leads in this case to the production of carbonate
such as calcite or dolomite which would also buﬀer the pH.
• Silica is produced from the weathering of silicate rocks although it might
not be signiﬁcantly correlated to EC as it is partially taken by the secondary
clay mineral residue kaolinite.
• Low Cl−concentrations don’t balance Na+ due to low occurrence of Cl in
the rock composition.
• K+ is a minor constituent due to low weathering potential of K-feldspar.
• Calcium plagioclase has high weathering potential reﬂecting the higher Ca2+
concentrations.
HCO−
3 does not seem to covary with the boreholes piezometric heads (Figure
5.8a) and depths (Figure 5.8b). It can therefore be concluded that the length of
the ﬂow path (oriented towards the river) is not expected to play a major role in
the groundwater chemistry as well as groundwater recharging deeper boreholes
don’t have generally undergone more interaction with the rocks. As the ﬂow rate
is relatively low and heterogeneous in hard rock aquifers, groundwater interaction
with the host rocks can change locally.
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.8: HCO−
3 against piezometric levels and depths of the boreholes.
NO−
3 concentrations are usually low ([0.1 ; 33.05] mg/L, median of 3.73 mg/L)
although it exceeds 10 mg/L in 6 boreholes. This is likely due to leakage of human
==PAGE:67==
5.4 Groundwater chemistry
57
wastewater due to the absence of sewage system and as agricultural activity and
the use of fertilizers are of low occurrence. NO−
3 would be mostly stable consider-
ing the O2 concentrations and redox potentials (Appelo and Postma, 2010). The
amount of dissolved organic carbon (DOC) among boreholes is low (<2.84 mg/L).
According to the redox conditions, sulfur (S) is exclusively present as SO2−
4
(Appelo and Postma, 2010) although the concentrations of SO2−
4
are low vary-
ing within [0.1 ; 11.1] mg/L, median: 1 mg/L. The highest concentrations could
reﬂect the local oxidation and dissolution of sulphide minerals. The low concen-
trations of Cl−also reﬂect the low-Cl rainfall of continental origin (Smedley et al.,
2007).
5.4.2.2
Dug wells
The groundwater from dug wells is acidic to neutral (pH in the range [5.05 ; 7.28])
and shows oxidizing conditions with O2 varying in the range [0.81 ; 3.9] mg/L
and Eh in the range [255 ; 305] mV. Figure 5.6 shows that the groundwater is of
Ca-Na-Mg-HCO3 type although one well is found with higher concentrations of
Cl−(i.e. 52.1 mg/L against [1.74 ; 4] mg/L). The TDS concentrations vary in a
wider range [42.9 ; 315.25] mg/L than for boreholes.
The deepest dug well (i.e. 13.3 m) has a neutral pH and major anions con-
centrations similar to the boreholes. Thus, groundwater found in it has certainly
undergone interaction with primary silicate minerals.
Lowest TDS and acidic pH can be explained by low contact of the shallow
groundwater with primary silicate minerals and by the low weathering potential
of secondary silicate minerals (i.e. kaolinite) forming the weathered overburden
(Appelo and Postma, 2010). Indeed, the two shallowest wells (i.e. 7.7, 6.7 m)
have relatively low concentrations of Si, HCO−
3 , Ca2+, Mg2+. The lower potential
for buﬀering the pH and the equilibrium with atmospheric CO2 lead to acidic
conditions. One of these shallowest wells has high TDS due to presence of elevated
NO−
3 and Cl−concentrations: 51.33 mg/L and 52.1 mg/L respectively. This well
was particularly found in a small agricultural area located nearby habitation.
Thus, the presence of NO−
3 could be explained by waste water leakage while high
Cl−would be due to higher evapotranspiration and dry deposition (Appelo and
Postma, 2010). Note also that the NO−
3 concentration of 51.33 mg/L exceeds the
WHO guideline of 50 mg/L.
==PAGE:68==
58
5 Results
5.4.2.3
Basins
The surface water from the basins are oxic, basic and of Ca-Na-Mg-HCO3 type.
The major species composition shows slight diﬀerences with groundwater as hav-
ing lower Ca2+ and HCO−
3 concentrations.
According to the batch experiments performed with PHREEQC (shown in
Appendix G), the increase in pH and decrease in major species is explained by
degassing of CO2 occurring through equilibrium with the atmosphere. This de-
gassing leads to the precipitation of carbonate minerals linked with the decrease
of HCO−
3 .
The relatively high concentrations of SO4 in basins B1 and B2 suggest an im-
portant dissolution of sulphide minerals. An accumulation of SO4 in the basins
would also be likely according to the high rate of evaporation and as this specie
does not seem to undergo transformation at new conditions considering the batch
experiments. However, concentrations in basin B3 were relatively low and com-
parable to the ones measured in groundwater. Therefore, the ﬁrst hypothesis is
favoured.

guideline and only two boreholes have concentrations exceeding 50 µg/L. More-

the dominant form of arsenic is arsenate (As(V)) against arsenite (As(III)).

computed As ﬂow paths (Figure 5.18) and on a geological map (Figure 6.2).
5.4.3.1
Other trace elements
The concentrations of trace elements are usually very low in all water bodies.
In particular, the species associated with Au gold mineralization and, thus, to
As-bearing sulphides such as Sb, W, Mo (Smedley et al., 2007) are low although
W can reach up to 16.89 µg/L which is an unusual concentration (Smedley et al.,
2007). Their relationships with As concentrations are discussed in Section 6.
Total iron concentrations are generally very low (median: 0.01 mg/L) but are
found in higher concentrations in 4 boreholes (i.e. [0.22 ; 2.95] mg/L). Consider-
ing the mildly reducing conditions and pHs with the Fe redox diagram, Fe2+ can
be stable locally. Indeed, Figure 5.9 shows that highest concentrations of Fe are
found for low Eh, pH and O21. Under this conditions, it could be assumed that
high Fe concentrations mostly correspond to Fe2+. This was also shown by the
speciation calculation performed with PHREEQC.
Moreover, signiﬁcant sources of Fe are expected to be only local, corresponding
to oxidation and dissolution of sulphide minerals (Smedley et al., 1996). However,
according to Figure 5.9d, no correlation is found between SO4 and Fe. Thus, high
Fe concentrations could be due to reductive dissolution of iron oxides whereas
high SO4 concentration with low Fe concentrations could result from the precipi-
tation of Fe into hydroxides under oxic conditions. Also note that the highest Fe
concentration is almost equal to the WHO guideline and corresponds to the well
PO-02 located hundred meters far from PO-01 which has an high As concentra-
tion.
1Eh and O2 were not measured from the previous sampling campaign, from which some
high Fe concentrations were found. Therefore, the plots Fe versus Eh and Fe versus O2 are
incomplete.

5.5
Hydrogeological modeling and transport of
arsenic
5.5.1
Hydraulic conductivity zonation
The performance of the hydrogeological model by decomposing the domain into
zones of equal hydraulic conductivities was evaluated. Figures 5.10a and 5.10b
show that the obtained ﬁt between simulated and observed heads is poor. This
can also be observed through the calculation of the high RMSE and MAE (i.e
7.1 and 6 m respectively). Moreover, according to Figure 5.11, some boundaries
are marked by abrupt hydraulic conductivity changes which does not seem to be
realistic as the hydraulic conductivity distribution of the ﬁssured layer is more
likely to be smoothed.
(a) Simulated vs observed heads.
(b) Residuals of all observation wells.
Figure 5.10: Plots representing the ﬁt between observed and computed heads.
The geological map used to deﬁne the diﬀerent zones might constitute a too
simplistic representation of the lithology on this scale and an accurate description
of the hydraulic property heterogeneity cannot be expected for this application.
Indeed, only few major faults are mapped while other lineaments might exist,
from which minor brittle shear zones are expected to play a major role in the
heterogeneity of the hydraulic conductivity.
Indeed, as discussed above these
features normally enhance the weathering process in their vicinity resulting in a
higher density of fractures in their neighbourhoods.
In order to accurately deﬁne zones of similar hydrodynamic properties, in par-
ticular for the ﬁssured layer, elaborate ﬁeld work for data collection (i.e. hydraulic
==PAGE:73==
5.5 Hydrogeological modeling and transport of arsenic
63
conductivity measurements), a detailed database or precise knowledge about the
structural geology and the lithology would be required (Ahmed et al., 2008).
Figure 5.11: Distribution of the hydraulic conductivity assigned to the ﬁssured
layer [m/s].
5.5.2
Pilot points approach
Using the inverse modeling process together with the pilot points approach, a
much better ﬁt between observed and computed heads is obtained (Figure 5.12).
The maximal individual error is 1.8 m while the ME, MAE and RMSE are -0.05,
0.65 and 1.15 m, respectively. This conﬁrms the very good agreement between
the computed and observed heads and shows that the model does not have the
tendency to underestimate or overestimate the hydraulic heads.
Table E.2 also displays the RMSE and MAE between individual computed
and observed heads for diﬀerent model calibrations. It can be seen that the model
performs the less well for head at PO-21.
Figure 5.13 shows that the spatial K distribution of the ﬁssured layer is
smoothed and no presence of bull’s eye is found over the model domain.
K
varies in the range [1.0E-7 ; 4.7E-4] m/s with a geometrical mean and a geomet-
ric standard deviation of 3.2E-6 m/s and 3.56 respectively. Thus, the optimized
==PAGE:74==
64
5 Results
(a) Simulated vs observed heads.
(b) Residuals of all observation wells.
Figure 5.12: Plots representing the ﬁt between observed and computed heads.
parameters don’t reach the upper bounds ﬁxed for the calibration while the ge-
ometrical mean points out that K is also skewed to its lower interval. The low
geometric standard deviation points out that, over the model domain, the ﬁs-
sured layer K slightly varies.
Figure 5.13 also allows the comparison between the lithology and the K dis-
tribution of the ﬁssured layer by superposition of the K distribution and the
geological map. It can be ﬁrst inferred that the K distribution is similarly ori-
ented with the structural geology in the N-S direction2. Hypothesis about the
relationship between the geological entities and the K distribution can also be
formulated. For instance, K seems to be highest along the hydrothermalized an-
desite and lowest in the rhyolite and rhyodacite. Volcano-sedimentary schist also
appears to exhibit high K. This could be due to the typical well-conserved weath-
ered zone in these formations, protected from erosion due to the development of
a thick weathered overburden (Koïta et al., 2013a; Kote et al., 2003). However,
as discussed above, the geological map accuracy is likely too low for a satisfying
comparison. Indeed, Figure 5.13b exhibits other faults.
Figure 5.14 represents the head distribution obtained after calibration of the
parameters. The groundwater ﬂows toward the Mouhoun river and the quarries
seem to act as sinks on the groundwater ﬂow.
2As discussed in Section 3.3, the structural geology is the result of the intrusion of plutons
on both East and West sides of the study area have led to a general lengthen of the geology
and a system of faults primary oriented North to South.

5.5.3
Model uncertainty
Figure 5.15 displays the K distributions of the ﬁssured layer obtained from 10
calibrated models as well as their respective ﬁnal objective function value Φg
expressing the degree of convergence. Φg of Solutions 4 and 6 showed that the
calibration converged badly for these simulations regarding Φg = 20 obtained for
the initial model previously described. Thus, solutions 4 and 6 are rejected while
the 9 other solutions are considered for the predictive uncertainty analysis. Note
that Φg is calculated from the diﬀerence between observed and computed heads
and is therefore correlated with the RMSE calculated for the corresponding sim-
ulation.
Signiﬁcant trends in the distributions can be observed between models with
satisfying convergence.
The range of K for the converging solutions is about
[1E-7 ; 5E-4] m/s. However, while the geometrical means of 8 of the converging
solutions are in the range [1.3E-6 ; 6.8E-6] m/s, solution 2 exhibits a geometrical
==PAGE:76==
66
5 Results
Figure 5.14: Simulated head distribution using pilot points and the Kriging in-
terpolation. Scale bar in [m].
mean of 3E-5 m/s. This diﬀerence can also be seen in Figure 5.15b with an overall
increase in hydraulic conductivity although the general pattern in relative K
distribution is preserved. Thus, this solution is not expected to present signiﬁcant
change in the predicted groundwater ﬂow path whereas it would induce diﬀerences
in the residence time of the groundwater.

Figure 5.16 displays the distribution of the geometric standard deviation of hy-
draulic conductivities using the 9 solutions presenting a good convergence. The
variation in hydraulic conductivity for similar locations between the diﬀerent
model is in general relatively low with a median geometric standard deviation
and mean geometric standard deviation of 3.9 and 4.2 respectively. However, at
discrete locations the variance in hydraulic conductivity can be high with the
geometric standard deviation reaching up to 14. The positions of the observation
wells are also generally correlated with zones where the hydraulic conductivity
varies the less. For instance, this is particularly visible in the vicinity of the 4
close observation heads in the East part of the model domain.
For all models considered as calibrated, the saprolite K varies in the range
[1.78E-8 ; 2.04E-6] m/s with a geometrical mean of 1.83E-7 m/s. The upper

bound of this range corresponds to the highest value of solution 2 which ex-
hibits an overall higher conductivity in the ﬁssured layer. The vertical anisotropy
(Kh/Kv) of the ﬁssured layer varies over the full range set for calibration [0.1 ;
10] with a median of 0.69. The upper bound of this range corresponds also to
the anisotropy obtained from Solution 2 while all values are below 1.14. The
low median value of the anisotropy shows, for all converging models, that the
ﬁssured layer tends to have higher vertical hydraulic conductivity which could be
accordance with the structural geology (i.e. vertical schistosity). The saprolite
Ks and the ﬁssured layer anisotropy are displayed in Table E.1
5.5.4
Sensitivity analysis
In order to allow a better comparison between the composite sensitivities of
the diﬀerent parameters, the normalized sensitivities are multiplied by a factor
1000. Among all converging models, the composite sensitivity of the ﬁssured layer
anisotropy is found in the range [0.27 ; 27.04] with a median of 3.65 and all values
below or equal to 7.03 except 27.04. The composite sensitivity of the saprolite K
is found in the range [1.27 ; 10.04] with a median of 4.92. The median composite
==PAGE:80==
70
5 Results
sensitivities for all parameters and for each simulation vary in the range [3.22 ;
5.32] with an average of 4.09. The median sensitivities of the saprolite K and the
ﬁssured layer anisotropy having their values close to the average median of all sen-
sitivities, it could be inferred that these 2 parameters are signiﬁcantly sensitive
to the model when considering all parameters independently. However, regard-
ing the number of total parameters (i.e. 193) and considering that the saprolite
and the anisotropy were homogeneously assigned over the model domain, their
inﬂuence are relatively low with respect to the pilot points that are individually
sensitive to the model for lower areas extent.
Figure 5.17: Median composite sensitivity of the pilot points represented by
graduated symbols divided in sextiles.
Figure 5.17 displays the relative median composite sensitivities and locations
of the pilot points with the use of graduated symbols. It can be observed that
zones where pilot points are of lesser sensitivity and, consequently, more likely
to have an hydraulic conductivity distribution poorly representing the reality are
correlated with areas where the density of observations is low. The latter is par-
ticularly the case over the West part of the model domain. However, according
to Figure 5.16, these zones are not correlated with areas exhibiting larger varia-
tions of hydraulic conductivities among the simulations. This can be due to the
fact that a) PEST utilities, as SVD-assist, hold parameters of low sensitivities
constant or/and b) the number of simulations is not suﬃcient to observe a cor-
relation between variations in hydraulic conductivity and pilot points with low
==PAGE:81==
5.5 Hydrogeological modeling and transport of arsenic
71
sensitivities.
5.5.5
Arsenic transport
Using the groundwater model developed with the pilot points approach, the trans-
port of arsenic from identiﬁed quartz veins was simulated to determine the zones
with potential contamination.
The diﬀerent As ﬂow paths generated forward
from the quartz veins and backward from the contaminated boreholes using the
9 converging solutions are shown in Figures 5.18 and 5.19 respectively.
Figure 5.18: Range of possible ﬂow paths for arsenic transport in groundwater
obtained by generating particles forwards from the quartz veins with distribution
of measured As concentrations.
From Figures 5.18 and 5.19, it could be observed that:
• the north part of the model domain is more likely to be impacted by oc-
currence of arsenic. This is primarily due to a majority of quartz veins
occurring in this zone.
• borehole PO-6 that was found contaminated is drilled across a quartz vein
and no ﬂow path ﬂowing from another potential source cross this borehole.
Therefore, this vein is likely to be an As source.

same As source although the model primary show a groundwater drainage
and the resulting particles ﬂow paths oriented toward B3. This implies that
the water level of quarry B3 is likely to be underestimated.
• The most aﬀected borehole PO-1 is located about 150-300 m far from two
quartz veins from which particles ﬂow paths cross this borehole. The parti-

is likely to be the main source of arsenic contaminating this borehole.

shows that As ﬂow paths are more likely to be oriented towards PO-1 rather than
