6.1
Arsenic
6.1.1
Chemical analysis
In order to study the spatial variability of arsenic, relationships between As and
physico-chemical parameters were ﬁrst identiﬁed by producing correlation plots
and by calculating R2 coeﬃcients shown in Figure 6.1.
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 6.1: Plots of As concentrations versus physico-chemical parameters and
species concentrations and R2 coeﬃcients. Circles: boreholes; Diamond: dug
wells ; Triangles: basins ; Dotted line: As WHO guideline (continued on next
page).
==PAGE:88==
78
6 Discussion
(e)
(f)
(g)
(h)
(i)
(j)
Figure 6.1: (continued)
Arsenic is not found to depend on the content of groundwater O2 concentra-
==PAGE:89==
6.1 Arsenic
79
tions as the latter are generally low and as high As occurs for O2 < 2 mg/L
(Figure 6.1a). In basins, high As concentrations are observed under oxic condi-
tions (i.e. O2>7 mg/L).
Arsenic seems to show some relationship with pH. It is particularly observed
for the basins exhibiting high As concentrations under basic pH and for acidic
groundwater having very low As concentrations. This can be explained by the
decreasing As(V) sorption into Fe oxide/hydroxides with increasing pH (Appelo
and Postma, 2010; Dixit and Hering, 2003).
As release does not seem to be primarily induced by reductive dissolution of
oxides/hydroxides. Considering the pH and O2 concentrations measured among
boreholes and regarding the Mn redox diagram, reductive dissolution of Mn does
not seem possible. Although highest As and Mn correlations were measured from
the same sample (Figure 6.1e) this might only result from the local oxidation of
Mn-rich silicates. Similarly, the predominance of oxic conditions limit the reduc-
tive dissolution of HFO and As does not seem not correlated with Fe (Figure
6.1f) although no measurements of the iron speciation were performed.
Arsenic shows some relationships with antimony (Sb) and molybdenum (Mo)
whereas As does not seem correlated with W (Figures 6.1g, 6.1h and 6.1i). Ac-
cording to Verplanck et al. (2008), Sb is related to the Au mineralization through
the sulphide mineral stibnite (Sb2S3). However, the Sb concentrations are found
very low and might therefore be impacted by measurements bias as well as it
is likely that Sb minerals are of low occurrence in Burkina Faso Au-mineralised
areas which is also suggested by Smedley et al. (2007).
Mo is present in ore
bodies as the sulphide mineral MoS2 and stable under aerobic neutral-pH as the
anion MoO2−
4 .
The good agreement between high As and Mo concentrations
as already observed by Smedley et al. (2007) in northern Burkina Faso support
the fact that As is derived from ore mineralization. W is usually present in the
ore bodies as wolframite ((Fe,Mn)WO4) and scheelite (CaWO4)) (Smedley et al.,
2007). The fact that Smedley et al. (2007) observed a relationship between As
and W in northern Burkina Faso contrary to the present study shows well that
the mineralization of the ore bodies highly varies. Similarly, no correlation was
observed between As and other trace metals linked with sulphide mineralization
(i.e. Cu, Ni, Zn, Co (Smedley et al., 1996)).
The relatively high R2 coeﬃcient between As and SO4 is mostly due to the
high concentrations occurring in the abandoned quarries. However, no apparent
correlation between these species is found in groundwater (Figure 6.1j) although
they are both produced by the dissolution of As-bearing minerals. As sulphate
==PAGE:90==
80
6 Discussion
is released by an higher number of sources as, for instance, the dissolution of
As-barren sulphides and/or leachate from the sewage system, thus, it is present
in much greater quantity (mg/L) than As (µg/L). However, As and sulphate con-
centrations in B1 and B2 are high supporting the hypothesis that large As-rich
sulphide mineral dissolution is occurring around their location and that these
species are transported towards these basins.
6.1.2
Lateral variability
From the simulated As ﬂow paths (Figures 5.18 and 5.20), the distribution of
As compared with the lithology (Figure 6.2) and correlations between As and
relevant trace elements, the spatial variability of arsenic seems to be primarily
controlled by the locations of the quartz veins. As the median As concentration
in boreholes is 2.95 µg/L, smaller sources of arsenic certainly exist over the entire
study area through diﬀuse sulphide mineralization in the host rocks. However, it
does not seem that large parts of the lithology as the hydrothermalized andesite
represent signiﬁcant sources of arsenic.
From the realistic distribution of hydraulic conductivity and the ﬁt between
some contaminated boreholes and generated ﬂow paths, it can be inferred that
groundwater ﬂow model can be developed on catchment scale to predict As risk
zones. However, in the absence of mining exploration, the localization of the
quartz veins is likely to be only partial. In the present work, the sources of ar-
senic were only deﬁned as quartz veins showing on the surface reported from one
author. The facts that a) borehole PO-6 was found to be drilled on an unmapped
quartz veins and that b) wells were found contaminated while not being crossed
by simulated particles ﬂow paths (i.e. PO-30, PO-28) reinforce the hypothesis
that quartz veins localization is incomplete. The management and the knowledge
of the ore resources are a sensitive matter and documents accurately quantifying
and locating these resources are usually limited to unpublished internal company
reports (Béziat et al., 2008). This represents a main limitation for the assess-
ment of As contamination and the application of a local mitigation strategy over
mineralized areas on catchment scale.
Diﬀerences in the magnitude of As concentrations among boreholes drilled
close to veins are also high. This is likely due to diﬀerences in the veins mineraliza-
tion. For instance, homogeneous milky massive white quartz was reported as not
containing any gold mineralization whereas grey quartz is more commonly associ-
ated with the ore deposit (Béziat et al., 2008; Gueye, 1984; Kabore, 1989). This
diﬀerence has been shown to be related to the structural type of the veins with the
==PAGE:91==
6.1 Arsenic
81
Figure 6.2: Lithology of the study area and distribution of arsenic concentra-
tion in groundwater and surface water found during the sampling campaigns of
February and April - May 2015 (adapted from Bamba (1996) and Ouédraogo
et al. (2003)).
Au-bearing veins having undergone deformation whereas Au-barren veins being
undeformed (Béziat et al., 2008; Gueye, 1984; Oberthür et al., 1997; Verplanck et
al., 2008). The orientation of the quartz veins could therefore be related to their
sulphide minerals content. Figure 6.2 shows that the veins are primarily oriented
==PAGE:92==
82
6 Discussion
in the N-S direction with the major lodes in the direction NW-SE. However, no
distinct orientation is found linked with the occurrence of high As concentrations.
Considering the general oxic and near-neutral pH conditions, arsenic is ex-
pected to be sorbed to Al, Fe or Mn mineral oxides/hydroxides or clays along
its transport (Smedley and Kinniburgh, 2002). Although reductive dissolution
of Fe hydroxides seems possible, its predominance as As source seems unlikely
regarding the scarcity of mildly reducing conditions and the poor correlation be-
tween As and Fe. Thus, contaminated wells should be found close to the sources
as hypothesized by Smedley et al. (2007), especially when considering the long
residence time of the water. This could explain the fact that PO-11 and PO-37
located in a simulated As ﬂow path and relatively far from quartz veins were
found uncontaminated.
6.1.3
Vertical variability
Smedley et al. (1996) and Smedley et al. (2007) suggested that the As concentra-
tions covary with the boreholes total depths as deeper borehole receives ground-
water having undergone more interaction with the rocks and, thus, having more
potential to be in contact with sulphides.
Arsenic does not seem to covary with HCO−
3 (Figure 6.1d) although high-
est As concentrations occur with high HCO−
3 concentrations (i.e. > 250 mg/L).
Correlation between As concentrations and boreholes total depths is not found
(Figure 6.1c)1. Moreover, HCO−
3 does not show any relationship with the bore-
holes total depths (Figure 5.8b). These results are therefore in disagreement with
Smedley et al. (1996) and Smedley et al. (2007).
Considering that the groundwater ﬂow is mostly horizontal and that the
quartz veins are vertical, the residence time of the water is not likely to con-
trol the As distribution through higher interactions with sulphides. First, deeper
boreholes could exhibit higher As concentrations only as a function of depth in-
creasing As-bearing sulphide minerals. Indeed, the minerals present at shallower
depth might have been more protected from weathering. Second, it is believed
that HCO−
3 concentrations is a function of both the residence time of groundwa-
ter and the interactions with silicate rocks. Positive correlation between HCO−
3
1Note that the depths weren’t obtained for all boreholes having high As concentrations.
However, some of the highest As concentrations are found for the relatively shallow boreholes
PO-30 and PO-36, 45.5 and 55.6 m deep respectively
==PAGE:93==
6.1 Arsenic
83
and As could exist through anions competition for sorption sites (COWI, 2005).
The vertical positions of the quartz veins together with the screen extensions
of the wells could also inﬂuence the distribution of As measurements. Although
all veins were considered as uniformly crossing the entire ﬁssured layer in order
to observe only the lateral contamination, veins can also be shallowly dipping.
In this case, the veins having limited vertical extension could generate arsenic
towards a close borehole without contaminating it if the screen is receiving un-
contaminated groundwater water from a diﬀerent ﬁssures set. Simulating the
vertical transport of arsenic would require to model the various ﬁssures set of
weathered bedrocks which does not seem realistic although distinct layers could
be deﬁned if a general behaviour in the fractures/ﬁssures density with depth is
noticed (e.g. depth decreasing ﬁssures density).
6.1.4
Dug wells
Arsenic concentrations found in shallow dug wells are all below 7.6 µg/L which
is consistent with Smedley et al. (2007) who hypothesized that these low concen-
trations are due to the short residence time of the groundwater in the weathered
overburden and to the capacity of As to be bound to secondary Fe oxides. Indeed,
as quartz veins are crossing the weathered overburden (Bamba, 1996), As can be
generated in groundwater within this horizon. However, the residence time of the
groundwater is not likely to play a major role in the groundwater composition as
this aquifer typically exhibits low conductivity, particularly in the saprolite layer
(i.e. capacitive function) and the groundwater would have time to undergone
interaction with the veins.
Goethite is found in the weathered overburden (i.e. ferricrete and saprolite)
oﬀering sorption sites for As at slightly acidic pH (Appelo and Postma, 2010;
Dixit and Hering, 2003) although goethite content decreases with depth (Bamba
et al., 2002). Similarly, kaolinite would also oﬀer sorption sites of lower signif-
icance and its content decrease with depth as the structure of the parent rock
is more conserved with depth. Thus, the shallowest dug wells drilled across the
horizon where large amounts of goethite are present seem to be preserved from
signiﬁcant As contamination.
Diﬀerently, groundwater of the deepest dug well seems to have undergone
interaction with primarily silicate minerals and exhibits the highest As concen-
trations. Thus, it is believed that signiﬁcant As concentrations can also occur
in dug wells depending on the well depth and the saprolite thickness due to the
==PAGE:94==
84
6 Discussion
decrease in goethite and kaolinite within the saprolite depth. This would also
depend on the alteration level of the sulphide minerals with depth as it can be
expected that sulphides are more consumed close to the surface (Bamba et al.,
2002).
6.1.5
Mining activity
The presence of current mining activity in the vicinity of Poura is exclusively per-
formed on small scale with the use of hand-dug tunnels and pits. Ore is manually
extracted from the quartz veins and disseminated gold is collected by rudimen-
tary tailings washing at various place around points of extraction (Figure B.5).
Whereas As groundwater pollution from tailings washing is not likely due to the
high content of Al and Fe oxides present in the weathered overburden strongly
sorbing As (Smedley et al., 2007), this work was observed on the banks of the
quarries B1 and B2 and could represent an input of As in surface water. However,
when comparing the density of this activity with the high As concentrations and
the size of the quarries, tailings washing is not likely to be the only responsible
for surface water pollution.
Gold washing could also pollute shallow dug wells through direct waste input
when performed in their vicinity as reported by Smedley et al. (2007) although
this was not observed in the ﬁeld. In addition, the application of polluting chem-
icals (i.e. Hg, CN−) facilitating gold extraction was not observed neither and,
therefore, not taken in the analysis although their use in Poura was reported by
Kafando (2012).
The main human pollution likely resides in the presence of abandoned quar-
ries and wastes disposal left from the past industrial activities. It can be assumed
that direct As transport to groundwater through rainwater percolation does not
occur due to sorption in the weathered overburden regarding the mining activ-
ities time scale (Smedley et al., 2007). However, waste disposals are likely to
pollute the surface water from direct runoﬀcarrying trace elements during the
rainy season with potential for acid mine drainage as observed by Kagambega
et al. (2014b).
The pollution of surface water from tailings dissolution is particularly ex-
pected for the quarries B1 and B2 considering their high As and SO4 concen-
trations1 as well as the presence of tailings on their edges whereas the landﬁll
might not represent a risk for surface water pollution as located too far from the
1The low SO4 concentrations in B3 show that the sulphate occurrence is not due to rainfall
input. Thus, high SO4 concentrations are expected to result from minerals dissolution.
==PAGE:95==
6.2 Model
85
basins. It could also be hypothesised that As and SO4 originates from quartz
veins and are transported through groundwater as the quarries are in a zone of
important gold mineralization and as they act as sink. However, when comparing
the expected total amount of groundwater ﬂowing into the basin and the amount
of groundwater ﬂowing through discrete quartz veins, arsenic and sulphate con-
centration would remain low through dilution.
During the rainy season, the groundwater system is expected to signiﬁcantly
change with a general increase of groundwater levels and surface water eleva-
tions. It could be supposed that the quarries turn into signiﬁcant As sources
for groundwater if the surface water levels exceed the groundwater levels during
occurrence of heavy rainfall. However, if the quarries don’t turn into a source for
the groundwater system, accumulation of As might be expected over time due to
the high evaporation.
Despite the fact that gold washing and mine waste disposal are not likely
to directly induce a groundwater contamination through eﬄuents percolation in
soils, it might lead to important soil pollution as observed by Kagambega et al.
(2014b). Other pollutants having an higher mobility might also leached from the
landﬁll to the groundwater.
Mining activities also represent a direct health threat. According to Kafando
(2012) and local inhabitants, the remnants of industrial mining as well as informal
gold mining are also sites of a signiﬁcant number of accidents. Injuries or deaths
regularly occur from the collapsing of unsecured abandoned hand dug tunnels or
as people fall in the basins due to crumbling of the cliﬀs. In addition, the quarries
are infested by caïmans that could represent a risk for the inhabitants working
nearby.
6.2
Model
The developed model and the applied calibration procedure based on pilot-points
have led to a good ﬁt between the modeled and observed heads together with
a smoothed distribution of the hydraulic conductivity. The uncertainty analysis
has also shown that the model is relatively robust considering the similarities in
K distributions among the various generated outputs although the results could
be implemented by gaining knowledge from further ﬁeld observations.
==PAGE:96==
86
6 Discussion
6.2.1
DEM and observed heads
As discussed in Section 5.1, the removal of noise from the raw SRTM DEM re-
sulted in a narrower range of elevations. This is of low impact on the piezometric
heads calculation and, consequently, on the hydrogeological model results as a)
these elevations correspond to features as scarps or quarries which are of rare
occurrence over the study area, b) piezometric heads are not calculated from wa-
ter levels measured at these locations simply because boreholes are not drilled on
steep slopes or hill tops, c) the areas of lowest elevations representing the quarries
can be disregarded as light reﬂection on water bodies or shadow are expected to
result in erroneous elevations in the raw SRTM DEM and as these water bodies
were redeﬁned in the hydrogeological model.
While random errors on measured water levels were optimally minimized (i.e.
dm accuracy) by testing variations and by performing measurements within a
short period, the systematic error on DEM elevations could signiﬁcantly aﬀect
the accuracy of the piezometric heads. Indeed, the smoothing process does not
increase the accuracy of the elevations which was evaluated by Slater et al. (2006)
to be less than 10 m for ﬂat areas. This potential error is high in comparison
with the range of terrain elevations and water levels, [228.50 ; 332.75] m and [5.95
; 15.88] m, respectively.
The obtained distribution of piezometric levels seemed, however, realistic and
allowed a converging calibration of the hydrogeological model although meter-
changes in observed piezometric levels might result in change in the predicted As
ﬂow paths. It would therefore be necessary to apply diﬀerent ﬁltering intensity
and to generate several model solutions with the various DEM produced in order
to test the prediction uncertainty related to the terrain representation. Accurate
ground control point elevations could also be measured in the ﬁeld at strategic
locations by using a static GPS in order to calibrate the DEM on these elevations.
6.2.2
Model uncertainty
6.2.2.1
Parameter uncertainty and local heterogeneities
The predictive uncertainty of the model due to parameters values was assessed
by computing 9 solutions with diﬀerent parameter sets whereas this analysis
should normally be performed using an higher number of independent simula-
tions (e.g.
thousand in Christensen and Cooley (2002)) in order to obtain a
representative statistical distribution of the solutions. As recommended by Ref-
sgaard et al. (2012), for this type of analysis, the model space should, however,
not be overpopulated and the number of poor and/or almost identical models
==PAGE:97==
6.2 Model
87
should be limited. Moreover, as performed in this study, simulations shouldn’t
be weighted when using them together to make predictions as a better converg-
ing model would not necessarily result in the best representation of As ﬂow paths.
Depending on the considered scale, the simulated spatial transport of As can
be more related to the modeled heterogeneities than on the parameters uncer-
tainty (Refsgaard et al., 2012). The allocation of pilot points regularly spaced by
500 m intervals as well as the regularization constraints and Kriging interpolation
were approximately deﬁned regarding the low density of observed data and in or-
der to obtain a smoothed representation of the ﬁssured layer heterogeneity on
catchment scale. Indeed, no detailed information about the spatial distribution
of the ﬁssured weathered bedrock heterogeneity could be obtained. This allows
the deﬁnition of high risk zones for As contamination although the model cannot
be expected to accurately simulate arsenic transport for smaller local-scale het-
erogeneities (i.e. 10th of meter). For instance, those could be taken into account
by reﬁning the spatial density of pilot points where (Doherty, 2003; Doherty et
al., 2010b):
• high density of the ﬁssured layer heterogeneities are expected
• accurate predictions have to be made
• higher density of observed heads is deﬁned
However, regular placement of pilot-points was chosen in order to easily in-
terpret the model output whereas use of an irregular distribution is a subjective
approach which would require better knowledge about the structural geology and
the intrinsic heterogeneity of the lithological units.
In order to restrain the range of parameter sets and to reduce the model
output error/uncertainty due to unmodeled local scale heterogeneity, it is of im-
portance to gain knowledge about the ﬁssured layer hydraulic conductivity. For
instance, this could be achieved by performing pumping tests at strategic loca-
tions for assessing the K distribution.
First, it would allow a more accurate
deﬁnition of the pilot points interpolation variogram by assessing the scale of
heterogeneity in order to allow a more realistic K distribution. Second, deﬁnition
of observed K values would result in restraining the range of K obtained from
the various simulations by ﬁxing pilot points values prior to calibration. Zones
where parameters sensitivities are low and where the K variance is high give an
estimation of locations where ﬁeld measurements should be achieved in order to
eﬃciently reduce the number of troublesome parameters and the range of possi-
ble parameter sets.
==PAGE:98==
88
6 Discussion
6.2.2.2
Geological structures
Another source of signiﬁcant prediction uncertainty and systematic error is ex-
pected to lie within the simpliﬁed geological structures. While accurate head
distribution can be achieved by compensation of the geological model structure
by the hydraulic parameters ﬁt, geological structure is expected to highly inﬂu-
ence the predictions beyond the scope of the calibration (Refsgaard et al., 2012).
Thus, the uncertainty on the arsenic transport prediction due to geological struc-
tures should be explored.
First, as As transport is expected to primarily occur within the ﬁssured weath-
ered bedrocks, this layer could be discretized for accounting the ﬁssures vertical
density variability. Second, the diﬀerent entities of the weathered overburden (i.e.
ferricrete, alloterite, isalterite) could be deﬁned. The latter would particularly be
of importance when using the model for rainy season in order to accurately model
the aquifer recharge. The development of these alternative structures would how-
ever require a better knowledge about the geology as the vertical frequency of
fractures remains unknown and as no clear saprolite composition could be deter-
mined. Refsgaard et al. (2012) also reported that a better discretization of the
geology would diminish the importance of local scale heterogeneity.
6.2.3
Conceptualization and observed parameters
One major systematic error is likely to lie in the deﬁnition of the water surface
elevations. In fact, these features were qualitatively deﬁned as constant head
and seem to have a signiﬁcant impact on the solution by draining the ground-
water and, consequently, aﬀect the As ﬂow paths. For instance, in the vicinity
of Bologo, the ﬂow paths were found exclusively oriented towards B3 although
groundwater in PO-36 was found contaminated with similar As concentrations.
In order to obtain accurate surface water elevations, static GPS measurements
should be performed in the ﬁeld.
Groundwater levels could not be measured for large zones of the study area.
This poor distribution impacted the model accuracy in two ways: First, the com-
puted heads in zones where no observed heads were deﬁned might be of poor
quality (i.e. due to low sensitivity of pilot points and variable modeled hydraulic
conductivity). This could particularly be the case in the southern and northern
extensions of the model domain whereas more weight is attributed on the calibra-
tion where high concentration of observed heads is found (e.g. north east part of
the model domain). Second, the model domain delimitation could be a signiﬁcant
source of systematic error as groundwater divide couldn’t be visualized for most
==PAGE:99==
6.3 Chemical measurements, sampling and analysis
89
of the boundaries. A better investigation would require additional drilling which
is not realistic regarding the costs as well as the logistical requirements implied.
Heterogeneities in the saprolite are also likely to exist as its composition is
related to the type of mother rock and the weathering process in a similar manner
as for the ﬁssure layer. As the saprolite is a porous material, its heterogeneities
are likely to be of less importance than for the ﬁssured layer. In addition, the
saprolite does not insure the transmissive function of the aquifer. Thus, lower
pilot points density could be deﬁned within this horizon (Doherty et al., 2010b).
Vertical interpolation between pilot points of the saprolite and of the ﬁssured layer
could be deﬁned (Doherty et al., 2010a) as these horizons are related through their
formations.
6.3
Chemical measurements, sampling and
analysis
The inherent hand-pumping systems of the boreholes were used to extract the
groundwater when collecting the samples used for average composition analysis.
This system could possibly let the groundwater interact with the atmosphere
leading to its oxidation and modiﬁcation of its chemistry (e.g. precipitation of
Fe2+. However, the ﬂow through cell and continuous O2 measurements insured
that a constant oxygen concentration was reached and as O2 was often very low,
insigniﬁcant interaction with the atmosphere is expected. Moreover, as ground-
water is extracted along the day, it is expected that stagnant water in the well is
constantly purged.
The number of basins and dug wells used for the analysis might be too low
in order to obtain accurate statistical representation for general interpretation.
This would also constitute a limitation for the analysis of high As occurrence as
a low number of boreholes were found contaminated.
Moreover, depth proﬁle analysis of the water composition performed with the
bailer was not conclusive due to a lack of boreholes data and to the potential
presence of numerous random errors when sampling (Section G).
6.4
Mitigation strategy
Considering the strong dependence for groundwater under the arid climate of
West Africa and the predictions of an increase water stress, it is crucial to insure
==PAGE:100==
90
6 Discussion
access to safe drinking water.
Mitigation strategies could be deﬁned on local and regional scales. Locally,
answers have to be adapted to the particularities of the ﬁeld, the detected con-
tamination variability and the irregularities in the As sources distribution. Re-
gionally, policies and programs should be adopted in order to face and limit As
contamination when developing new drilling programs. This could be applied
over risk zones deﬁned by statistical analysis (e.g. global risk maps) or corre-
sponding to the Birimian greenstone belts linked to As occurrence (COWI, 2005)
and covering an area of 70000 km2 in Burkina Faso (i.e. 10 % of the land area)
(Smedley et al., 2007).
As an example for local mitigation, the speciﬁc case of Poura is considered.
Arsenic concentrations exceeding the WHO guideline can be considered of low
occurrence (i.e. 17%) and, mainly, of low magnitude. Considering the low eco-
nomical resources and scarcity of groundwater, mitigation should only focus on
the closure or replacement of PO-1 and PO-30 presenting the highest As concen-
trations (317.5 and 51.9 µg/L respectively). The closest boreholes from PO-1
and PO-31 are respectively 200 m and 500 m far from them and could therefore
represent ﬁrst temporary replacement. Regarding PO-1, recommendations for
its replacement could be supported by the As distribution predictions discussed
above. Moreover, the inhabitants had a lot of interest in the sampling performed
on the ﬁeld, many oﬀered their help and were aware of our arrival. Indeed, hu-
man communication is very important in this region and could represent a crucial
vector for informing the population (e.g. prioritize the use of another well).
On regional scale, simple measures for arsenic mitigation could be proposed:
• Systematically analyse the groundwater quality when drilling and minimize
the placement of wells on or near quartz veins (require collection of geolog-
ical data and ﬁeld observations).
• Maximize the use of treated water surface supply.
• Close the contaminated boreholes and relocate groundwater abstraction
point. If the new location is too far for the population, shallow dug wells
could be developed although they are at higher risks for bacterial and other
contaminations.
• Provide centralized equipped zone for gold-washing in areas of intensive
hand dug mining (e.g. Bologo) and regulate this work by the deﬁnition of
standards.
==PAGE:101==
CHAPTER 7
Conclusion and future work
The spatial variability of arsenic in the gold-mining area of Poura was evaluated
through the development of a groundwater ﬂow and nonreactive conservative
transport model together with a geochemical analysis. Data collection was per-
formed through ﬁeld work during the dry season. Arsenic is found as As(V) and
most groundwaters have low As content (i.e.
< 10 mug/L) with the highest
concentration reaching 317 µg/L. Arsenic in groundwater exceeding the WHO
guideline for drinking water is primarily expected to be of geogenic natural oc-
currence through dissolution of sulphide minerals contained in the quartz veins.
Regarding the obtained As distribution together with the identiﬁed quartz veins
as well as the long residence time of groundwater, the contamination is expected
to be limited in the close vicinity of the veins locations. This results in a large
lateral variability of As occurrence controlled by the locations of the quartz veins
bearing sulphidic mineralization. The diﬃculty in locating As sources as well as
the change in the quartz veins mineralization and in the dissolution of As-bearing
minerals represent the main limitation to predict the distribution and magnitude
of the As groundwater contamination on local-scale. In addition, As concentra-
tions could increase as a function of depth due to the expected depth increasing
preservation of sulphides from weathering.
In this work, quantiﬁcation of As-travel distances and assessment of the geo-
chemical dynamics (i.e. description of the spatial and temporal evolution of the
transport) were not performed due to the low amount of available data. Similarly
to (Postma et al., 2007; Wallis et al., 2010, 2011), reactive transport models (i.e.
1D, 2D and/or 3D using the software PHT3D) coupled to a groundwater ﬂow
model (i.e. using MODFLOW) could be developed at a ﬁeld-scale to describe the
transport of arsenic in fractured media of West Africa. The area deﬁned as an
As-risk zone in the vicinity of the most aﬀected borehole (i.e. PO-1) represents a
trial site of interest for data collection. This would required a) pumping tests to
assess the hydrogeological heterogeneity and b) the drilling of monitoring bore-
holes to allow groundwater sampling and mineral assemblage (i.e. sediments and
cores) analysis as well as to gain knowledge about the geological structure.
The strong bimodal seasonal patterns shared between a dry and a rainy season
==PAGE:102==
92
7 Conclusion and future work
resulting in signiﬁcant hydrological and geochemical changes in the West African
region probably plays a major role in the pathways of arsenic in groundwater.
This expected mechanism, as for instance reported by Verplanck et al. (2008) in
Alaska, could be investigated through the study of the groundwater dynamics
(e.g. transient hydrogeological model), together with the observation of changes
in the geochemistry during the rainy season.
Because As contamination is highly localized, high-arsenic groundwater oc-
currence is not expected to be widespread in Burkina Faso. Indeed, considering
the present and previous surveys (COWI, 2005; Smedley et al., 1996, 2007), As
contamination is likely to be spotted in the many gold mineralized areas present
over the Birimian formations (Smedley et al., 2007). The development of a na-
tional statistical arsenic predictive risk map for Burkina Faso, as existing for
China (Rodriguez-Lado et al., 2013) and Korea (Ahn and Cho, 2012), could rep-
resent a powerful tool for public services in order to develop eﬃcient mitigation
strategies. Zones expected to be impacted by high-As occurrence in groundwater
should beneﬁt from speciﬁc drilling programs in order to insure drinking water
safety. On local scale, the positions of the known quartz veins and direction of
groundwater ﬂow should particularly be taken into account when installing new
boreholes and groundwater composition should systematically be evaluated when
drilling. Cooperation between the mining companies and the public services in
charge of providing access to drinking water could represent an eﬃcient strategy
for locating potential sources of arsenic.
As past mining activities also seem to lead to signiﬁcant As surface water
pollution through the dissolution of As-bearing sulphides present in the tailings,
remediation of abandoned sites should be achieved. Furthermore, considering
the apparent level of risks of traditional hand dug mining and the potential use
of chemicals such as Hg and CN’, this activity should be regulated by eﬃcient
policies to insure safety for the workers and to limit potential anthropogenic pol-
lution.

APPENDIX B
Field Work and observations
Figures B.1 and B.2 show part of the equipment used during ﬁeld work. The
choice of the bailer rather than a pump was made regarding ﬁeld practical issues
(i.e.
temperature, ease and rapidity of use, accessibility of the boreholes and
wells). Figure B.2 shows how the measurement of the EC, Eh, pH and T pa-
rameters were performed in bores. By using the inherent pumping system of the
borehole and by maintaining a constant pressure in the pipes by hand-pumping,
a stabilization of the parameters could be reached. Also, some groundwater lev-
els were measured while pumping in order to assess if hand-pumping resulted in
signiﬁcant groundwater drawdown.
Figure B.1: Part of the material used for measurements and sampling in the ﬁeld.
==PAGE:114==
104
B Field Work and observations
Figure B.2: Measurements of the EC, Eh, pH, T using ﬂow-through cell.
Figures B.3 and B.4 display document used on the ﬁeld to facilitate the sam-
pling and measurements procedure.
Figure B.3: Example of a map indicating the locations of boreholes. The target
and the tick represent the wells to be and already sampled respectively.
==PAGE:115==
B Field Work and observations
105
Figure B.4: Field analyses sheet for one borehole. The ﬁrst table aims to list
the technical characteristics of the borehole when available. The second table is
used to write the parameters read on the multimeter when stabilization is reach
using the ﬂow through cell for analyses of the average groundwater composition.
The third table is used to write the parameters read on the multimeter for each
discrete selected depth interval when performing multilevel sampling.
==PAGE:116==

B Field Work and observations

speciﬁc chemicals to facilitate gold extraction wasn’t observed.
Figure B.5: Location of gold washing on the banks of the abandoned quarries.

APPENDIX C
Database
This Appendix presents all technical data relative to the wells gathered during the
study. Table C.1 contains the coordinates of the boreholes and dug wells in the
districts of Poura and Fara. For each well used in the present study, Tables C.2,
C.3, C.4 display the ID, the location (i.e. name of the village, coordinates), the
height obtained from the DEM, the type of well (i.e. borehole or dug well), the
type of pump, the year of construction, the measured water level, the total depth,
the obtained water level from the literature and the corresponding month of
measurement, the converted piezometric level from the measured water level, the
thickness of the ﬁssured layer, the thickness of the weathered overburden deduced
from the thickness of the ﬁssured layer and the total depth of the borehole, the
depth of the top position of the screen or the depth interval of the screen. Table
C.5 shows the thickness of simpliﬁed horizons deduced from detailed lithologs for
a larger area extent than the considered study area.

==PAGE:165==
G Chemical measurements
155
Tables G.5 and G.6 display the chemical measurements performed at speciﬁc
depths in the 5 boreholes selected for the chemistry multilevel analysis. However,
the groundwater ﬂow line that was assumed to pass through these boreholes at
the time of ﬁeld work was found incorrect by comparing the positions of these
boreholes and the hydrogeological model result.
As the groundwaters in PO-
01 and PO-06 are found contaminated, the depth proﬁle analysis was therefore
restrained to these two boreholes. Figures G.1 and G.2 show the depth proﬁles
of relevant chemical species measured in PO-1 and PO-6 respectively.

==PAGE:170==

G Chemical measurements
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
(e)
Figure G.1: Depth proﬁles of representative chemical species in borehole PO-1.
The horizontal dotted lines represent the top and bottom of the well screen.
The groundwater in PO-1 and PO-6 are both oxic with O2 concentrations
above 1 mg/L. Figure G.1 shows that the composition of the groundwater in PO-
1 is relatively stable with depth whereas Figure G.2 shows more changes in the
groundwater composition of PO-6 with depth. In particular, the concentration
of arsenic is lower than the WHO guideline value for shallower depth and exceeds
it at depth of 70 m.
==PAGE:171==
G Chemical measurements
161
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
(e)
Figure G.2: Depth proﬁles of representative chemical species in borehole PO-6.
The position of the screen was not found for this borehole.
The increase in As concentration with depth in PO-6 seems to be correlated
with an increase in Mo and SO4 which could show a dissolution of sulphide min-
erals speciﬁcally at deeper level. However, the very low concentrations of Mo
don’t allow to conclude accurately about this relationship. Moreover As concen-
==PAGE:172==
162
G Chemical measurements
trations seems to be negatively correlated with species (Fe, Mn, Al) that oﬀer
sorption sites under oxyhydroxides forms although the chemical analysis don’t
oﬀer details about the speciations of these species. The higher concentration of
HCO3 at deeper level in PO-6 is not likely to play a role in the As increase though
competition for sorption sites. Indeed, HCO3 concentrations are high along the
entire water column and lower concentrations at shallowest level could be due to
CO2 degassing due to the contact with the atmosphere.
The absence of knowledge about the screen positions in the boreholes during
the time of ﬁeld work represent the main limitation for interpretations of these
results. In the case of PO-1, the screen is relatively short and only one sample can
be considered representing fresh groundwater entering the bore. In the case of
PO-6, it is not possible to determine if the sampled groundwater represent aquifer
conditions (i.e. dynamic groundwater entering the bore) or stagnant groundwa-
ter.
The action of retrieving the pumping system and of lowering and withdraw-
ing the grab sampler might also lead to mixing and agitation of the groundwater
column, to mobilization of previously aquifer matrix materials, to resuspension of
sediments and to aeration of the groundwater (Nielsen and Nielsen, 2006). This
was particularly observed in borehole PO-7 in which the Fe hydroxides were re-
mobilized. As the boreholes are constantly used by the population along the day,
no time could be spent for natural conditions recovery.
These measurements limitations are sources of random and systematic errors
which are likely to result in numerous misinterpretations. Therefore, it is not
possible to conclude about the change in groundwater composition with depth.
Considering the time required for and the complexity of this analysis, this study
requires speciﬁc ﬁeld work for which the following recommendations can be for-
mulated:
• The screen position should be known prior to perform gab sampling.
• The well should be purged after retrieving the inherent pumping system
in order to remove the remobilized particles that were ﬁxed to the tubing
system.
• Sampling should be repeated in time to evaluate the presence of random
error.
• Measurements should be performed in order to evaluate the speciation of
the species involved in the As sorption mechanisms (Fe, Mn, Al).
